Messrs. Beale & Co.’s Great Enterprise.
mHERE can be no quvs’iojiing the fact that the Piano
J Trade of these Colonies lias undergone a complete
revolution during the last five years ; and to that desirable
change certainly no firm lias contributed more than
Messrs. Heale and Company. During the time high prices
continued the trade was held by a few, and hundreds of
homes now remhwed attractive by the glorious influence
of music scarce con'd dream of becoming - the ultimate
owners of the instrument which has done so much to
cheer and to refine our modern domestic life.
When Beale and Company commenced business they
had quietly gauged this condition of affairs in Australia,
and they began operations on a plan which could alone
work out successively in a mammoth undertaking such as
they proposed. With the Australian selling branch of
their enterprise they resolved to combine the manufacture
of Pianos in Germany—the centre of the world’s trade for
that instrument. But the experts sent out here for the
purpose of fully determining what was best fitted for
colonial requirements reported that it was essential to
have a special make of piano to suit our climate—an in-
strument as different as those made for Europe as Aus-
tralia is different from the Old World. The result of this
was the production of the staunchest pianoforte of
modern times—the iron-framed “ Hapsburg.” The in-
strument is regarded by the most capable experts of
Europe as the Production of the Age, combining as
if .docs every feature of the highest class instrument with
the maximum of strength and durability, and it was placed
on the market at prices much under what other firms and
manufacturers had previously demanded. Although it
took almost endless trouble and long, patient labour to
produce t he design of the “ Inimitable Hapsburg,” yet once
produced the demand grew so rapidly, and has become so
great, that the enterprising originators arc able to out-
distance all competitors for both quality and price. The
peculiar construction of the iron frame, the excellence
of the wires, and the unique movements have, with all
other factors, contributed to this splendid success. No
other instrument possesses to the same degree such rich
full, clear, hell-like tones, so noticeable m the “Haps-
burg,” especially in the higher grades, while strength,
solidity, and imperviousness to climatic influences hove
been so well looked to that whether on me coldest o eak-
est spots of New Zealand or our own Monaro Plains, or in
the hottest of our Western and Northern areas, this
wonderful pianoforte not only maintains, but improves its
tone and consequent value even after years of use. And
it must continue so. The genius and experience of the
mechanical world in this branch of art arc embodied i .
this one instrument—the inimitable “ Hapsburg a d
the sales to-day give ample evidence of the repute in whi h
it is held. Beale Sc Company have placed Hie
“Hapsburg” with thousands of families in Australia.
Homes in the far-distant hack blocks, and homes in the
city, arc not only enlivened but are made purer and better
through the influence of its music. The firm resolved to
do a large trade by selling these superior instru-
ments at prices and on terms demanded for the
lower grades, and success has crowned their efforts.
They do by far the largest business, and hold the
largest stocks in the colonies. They give their best
efforts to suit the circumstances and requirements of
every customer, and they supply for cash or on an easy
system of Time-payment which they have made peculiarly
their own. The correspondence department is of itself a
huge undertaking; every mail brings its burden of letters.
From the great landowner, from the rich squatter, and
the astute merchant they come. And they came too
from clergymen, from doctors and solicitors, from civil
servants, and from the struggling selector, the tradesman,
the workingman ; for fathers and mothers of every grade
and class now recognise the influence which music exerts
for good in the life of the home. And every writer re-
ceives courteous attention and immediate response.
Hapsburg Double Iron-framed Pianos
Possess the following Advantages -
Ist.—-The wires are of a very high quality and in-
tensely sensitive; their vibrations, reverberated
by a sounding board of special construction, are
clear and bell-like, instead of the muffled, tin-
kettly, or metallic sounds so common in cheap
Pianos,
2nd.—Being double oblique the strings are. of a
greater length and fuller tone than in the old
straight-strung Pianos.
3rd. —The action is perfect check repeater, and is
made of pencil cedar, an expensive material,
and best for resisting climatic changes.
4th.— All the Hapsburg Pianos are Trichord.
•Jth. —They are all seven Octaves,
titli,—They have consolidated Iron Frames of
great strength, thus resisting the rapid muta-
tions of a tropical climate, whereby tuning is
m iuired at long intervals.
They oppose obstacles to the inroads of cock-
roaches, silver-fish, mice, &c. They have v\ ire
gauze backs which cannot be eaten by vermin,
and other unique improvements devised for
suniliar ends,
oth.—The Pelts are Clinched on the Hammers,
instead of being fastened only by glue, as is.the
case with nearly every Piano except the Haps-
burg.
•'Mi.-... They have brass nr nickel-faded 'VV rest-
Planks, instead of the- old-fashioned porHiable
. 'style,
•dlth. - -Rust, is guarded again.-b wherever possible,
Hr if substitution of bronze for other metals,
—tli.—.The outer eases are handsome in design,
and of durable beautiful woods; they can be
bad in walnut, or in walnut and gold, or in
gold.
—-No matter in what part of the colony you
> ve, we will supply you with a Hapsburg Piano
upon easy terms of payment. No charge will bo
made for packing or fur packing cases.

SATURDAY. MAY 17, 1890.
The Queensland Labour Difficulty.
•I ust now organised Australian Labour is
confronted with a difficulty which may
develope into one of the most desperate and
far-reaching struggles between employers
and employed that lias ever taken place in
this country. For some time past an in-
creasing degree of attention has been given
by the workers of the Northern colony to
the practical unification of their numbers in
order to secure the fullest possible return for
their toil. The (questions of Chinese and
kanaka labour, which have been growing
more momentous and urgent of late, have,
among others, had the effect of awakening
the wage-earners to the necessity for con-
solidation and enrolment in an organised
body. About the end of last year the
Queensland Labourers’ Union jointed hands
with the Australian Labour Federation
and made a determined attempt to resist the
efforts of the squatters to reduce the wages
of casual bush labour below the rate of 30s.
per week. The effect of this was that,
although notice of an intended reduction
.had been given by the employers, in almost
all cases the rate of wages was maintained.
The most notable instance of resistance to
what one would judge to be a very reason-
able demand was that of Mr. Tyson, a wool-
millionaire who liberally supports Christian
churches, Soudan Contingents, and cut-
throat competition. The Shearers’ Union,
however, which had that matter in hand,
with the promise of help from the Austra-
lian Labour Federation, showed its grit
so unmistakeably that Mr. Tyson came
down. But in the meantime the Dar-
ling Doyviis pastoralists, it is alleged,
decided to try their hands at com-
bination also,, and endeavour to turn an
honest penny by getting cheaper labour than
the Unionswvere inclined to supply. They
selected .londaryan Station as a test-place,
owing to its proximity to a rail way-station,
ami the consequent ease with which wool
could be transported from flic shed. This
move was to a, certain small extent success-
ful. After a world of trouble and perturba-
tion of spirit, the proprietors of .londaryan,
employing non-union labour but paying
union rates, managed to get their wool shorn
and delivered on the wharf at Brisbane for
shipment. But the Shearers’ Union called
in the assistance of the Australian Labour
Federation, and live wharf labourers, who arc
not connected with the Federation, promised
to assist, as also did the Queensland Sea-
men’s Union. Delegates from these bodies,
representing a formidable labour power, met
together and decided upon the next step,
which was to warn the British and India
Steamship Company against carrying to
market the Jondaryau wool, on pain of the
probability of their being involved iu serious
labour disputes not only in Australia but
in England. The company’s reply was con-
sidered evasive, and they were called upon
to definitely abandon evasion and state ex-
plicitly by a given date whether or no they
would pledge themselves not to carry as
freight wool shorn at non-union sheds.
Ultimately it was decided that a conference
of representatives of all parties con-
cerned should meet and discuss the matter
fully. It is generally thought that an ami-
cable settlement will be arrived at, but the
labour party are evidently determined to
fight vigorously in case it is not. Mr.
Crawford, a representative of the Federa-
tion, told a Sydney interviewer that the
Shearers’ Union was prepared to supply a
mounted force of 5000 men to patrol the
borders of Queensland and keep out non-
union labour, if the worst should ultimately
happen. This sounds like a large order, but
the compactly-organised Shearers’ Union has
a large and determined army of members
from which to draw scouts.
* # *
While The Bulletin entirely sympathises
with the unionists iu their effort to prevent
wages from going below the present Pumsoll
mark—while it applauds the organisation
which enables men to demand, instead of
beseeching, something like reasonable pay-
ment for their services—there are some
things in connection with this movement
which cannot but be deplored. One of these,
and the principal, is the issue of wildly-
written manifestoes by the Australian Feder-
ation which cannot fail to bring ridicule upon
an organisation whose object is of the highest,
and which should be so conducted as to com-
mand respect as well as success. The official
utterances of such a body as the Federation
should be calm, temperate, and logical. The
only enduring way to effect reforms is by
setting forth clearly what is the nature of the
change desired and how it may best be
accomplished. It is only because of the
force with which it appeals to reason and
judgment that any movement should succeed.
Yet here is a slab from one of the manifes-
toes of the Australian Labour Federation :> —
The principle we fight for will, if won, save our children
from the degradation that covers other lands, and creeps
already on our Australia. It is the golden key to the
rights of labour. It is the key that workers have lost for
long ages and which, having found again, they should
guard as dearer far than life. Fellow-unionists, our
cause is a sacred one ; it is holy ; it is the hand-grip of
organised toilers standing in one solid mass between
I>i - others whom they have never seen and tyranny which
they have felt and know to he unclean ; and because it is
so sacred and so holy, because it is an inspiration of the
Humanity within us and the voice of the Unknown God
who speaks by the voice of the people, we call upon you
to act like nieu who have faith awl hope, not like brute
beasts, who only despair. We seek no combat, but if
combat is forced upon us, we beg of you, as men to men,
to cast no stain upon the sacred cause of labour. You
will light for the weak against the strong, for the little
ones not yet born against the tyranny that tills the world
with weal and woe and shame.
And here is another ;
Those who fight will he fed by those who do not fight
Through a throbbing continent funds and friends a-e to
he found, and if the worst come to the worst, then tighten
your belts and tight on still quietly, still orderly, still
soberly. Remember that we fight for laws against the
lawless, that we fight with the day of justice dawning
before our eyes, with the current of human events sweep-
ing us on.
# * #
This is the sort of thing that makes the
average white man’s gorge rise, and induces
the thought that the literary department of
the Federation is controlled by “ scab ”
workers. It is unalloyed slush. It is simply
rant, without a gleam of coherence or argu-
ment showing through its turgid depths.
The “ Unknown God ” who inspired such
stuff has very little sense, and does not un-
derstand the English language. There is no
such thing as a “throbbing continent” out
here, and those who fight will only be fed
by those who do not fight on condition that
they know what their own interest in the
combat amounts to. Disordered appeals to
“ unknown gods” and flatulent outbursts
of rabid claptrap generally are not going
to advance a cause which is based upon solid
principles of right and equity. The Aus-
tralian Labour Federation should he sternly
censured by the well-wishers of labour
organisation for permitting such silly and
injurious things as wo have quoted to be
uttered under its sanction. These manifes-
toes read very differently from the clear
and common-sense statements of their case
made by the leaders of the London dockers’
strike, which elicited such a memorable
response from Australians. The Queens-
land workers will have every true man’s
sympathy and assistance if they show, as
they can readily do, that they have
a grievance ’which needs remedy. But
mere chaotic outpourings of feeling such
as we refer to, however laudable the
feeling or imperative the cause of it,
will not accomplish anything beyond the
discrediting of those who really deserve
thanks and praise for what they are trying
to do. All that needs to be said is that
capital and monopoly are seeking to place
labour at a further disadvantage iu order to
increase their own power and gains, and
whatever Australians can do to prevent
t hem will bedone. There is no need to become
hysterical or lapse into semi lunacy over it.
The Poor Heathen.
That shapeless and unfixable thing generally
alluded to as the “ native ” has served for so many
years as a rallying point for the pious enthusiasm
of the Anglo-Saxon race that it will be a cause of
genuine sorrow in many quarters to observe that
public interest in him seems to be somewhat on
the wane. The “ native,” it is true, was always a
somewhat hazy and indefinable institution. Very
few seemed to know exactly where he hung out,
though it was unanimously agreed that he Was
somewhat short in the matter of clothing. It was
generally accepted that he resided in warm lati-
tudes, though how he got into them, or what he
did when he was in them, or what reasonable hope
there Was of getting him out of them again was
not Very well defined. It was decided that lie
habitually sat on the shore and read tracts written
in English, and understood them in his own
language, and that he pined for the arrival of a
missionary above all other earthly considerations,
and that the next tiling lie longed after particu-
larly was to be annexed, and have a church built
in Isis immediate vicinity. These cherished
theories Were so deeply rooted that many orthodox
believers, even when the genuine aboriginal rose
before them, refused to recognise him, and still
kept on subscribing for the spread of the Gospel
in the land of that shadowy native who could
never be found—the mild, yearning, brown person
who wanted to hand over his last cocoa-nut to the
good cause, and be at rest. But within the last
few years various wandering iconoclasts have
arisen to denounce tins fanciful native as a
delusion and a myth, and it lias been more than
hinted that the average missionary, having failed
to find the gentle heathen who had pined for the
Scripture, has simply seized upon the heathen of
real life, and tried to make him assume a receptive
frame of mind by the application of personal
violence. Only a few weeks ago, the South
Australian Minister of Education blasphemously
declared his opinion that the orthodox native in
that province Was worse than the heterodox native
of long ago, and hinted that it might be as well
to let the aboriginal have an interval of repose
during which ho might possibly assimilate the
doctrines he had already acquired and get ready
to hold some more at a future date.
The South Australian Churches, however, in-
dignantly repudiate the insinuations levelled
against them, and as they unanimously deny that
there is nothing wrong with their ministrations
tiie probabilities are that they are experimenting
on the wrong kind of heathen and trying to fit an
angular savage into a purely circular doctrine.
The Australian blackfellow, it seems, is an un-
promising subject. “Man,” according to Gil-
bert, “ at best is only a monkey shaved,” but
the blackfellow was not properly shaved at the
beginning of his career ; Nature merely tore some
of the superfluous hair off him, and then turned
him loose in the highway of zoology to take his
chance. The primieval Mongolian explorer, who
visited these, shores in his jffnk, found this patchy
individual waiting in an unfinished state on the
shore, and probably he did his best to confer upon
him the blessings of civilisation, so that the native
might ultimately east the shadow of his huge
shock-head athwart the pages of history and
wriggle insanely in the van of the great army of
progress. He expounded to him, it may be, the
advantages of that narrow path which would ulti-
mately lead him to spend an eternity with Joss
Confucius in a paradise which was an outrage on
perspective, and told him of the glories of a
Flowery Laud above 1 and the native, to judge by
the few remaining signs of that Mongol invasion,
disregarded these teachings, and ate the teacher,
and burned the teacher’s junk, and left a blank
among the works of Buddha— the great first cause
who hangs out on top of his lotus-floWer in the
depths of space.
* # #
Then, the Christian settler followed in the track
of the vanished Mongol, and took stronger mea-
sures for the spread of the Truth. He shot a
great part of the shadowy native, and hanged a
good deal of the rest of him, and crippled or
otherwise damaged what was left, and the scared
remainder of the heathen departed ih a shapeless
cataract of arms and legs and hair into the distant
wilderness, and then the bearer of good tidings
started after him and bade him be of good cheer,
for he had got to bo saved or burst. The amount
of time and money and energy that was spent on
this good work is past computation, and the vigour
of the Christian teacher is a bright spot in Aus-
tralia’s history. He got the aborigine down and
showed him the matter in various new and unex-
pected lights. He fixed him with the hostile ex-
pression of his eye and explained to him about a
burning devil who performed unfathomable gyra
tions with a pitchfork in the trackless jungles of
theology. He followed him with a gun and un-
folded the doctrine of brotherly love, and naturally
enough the untutored savage concluded that the
gun and the communion of the saints were con-
nected in some way, and noted with interest that
the gun had a hole in the end of it and that the
hole seemed to go right through to the other end.
and thus gradually things got mixed up past all
recovery. Then the missionary would tie stray
members of his flock up to trees and fences, and.
reveal to them mysterious facts about people who
fell down dead in Clays gone by, and gradually
under this process the native acquired a few stray
ideas on different subjects and gained great advan-
tage thereby. He began to accept the fact that
somewhere beyond the fixed stars and on the
other side of the planetary system there dwelt a
being of great size who lasted a long time, and
who hit the bad nigger in a way that was awful to
think about; and he had a shadowy dream of a
future state in which he might become au im-
perishable blackfellow and throw an everlasting
spear at a kangaroo which would never pass away.
But somehow he never got beyond this point, and,
as a rule, lie only attained it to die shortly after-
wards. The white man’s theology was too under-
mining for him, and besides the missionary gave
way at times to a demoralising habit of firing bis
information into him with powder which promised
to end in the entire annihilation of the flock.
Everywhere the result has been much the same.
Even among line more peaceful and intelligent
tribes of the South Sea Islands the triumphs of
the faith have merely produced a state of things
iu which rival congregations grasp eaeli other by
the hair on minute points of theology, and pro-
spect for.each other with texts and axes and boil-
ing water and Confessions of Faith, until all is
chaos and general disaster.
# # *
Apparently the European teacher’s theology is
a misfit, and the attempt to crowd the unhappy
“ native ” into a creed which is too light for him
is merely hurrying him on the road to extermina-
tion. Moreover, he is required to hold too many
different kinds of doctrine, and he is apt to burst-
under the pressure. Iu England alone, five or six
years ago, some 233 rival churches were scrambling
for supremacy; several new ones have since been
added, and every one of them wants to annex the
unhappy “native,” generally without any fixed
idea of where he is to be found or what is the best
thing to do with him when he is met with.
America, too, has samples of all these churches
and some thirty or forty more that are exclusively
her own. Some of them exist by having fits during
service, and others have convulsions instead. One
maintained a precarious existence for a time on the
ground that Napoleon I. was still alive, and
broke down at last because it could not decide
what was the best use to put him to. Another
arose because it thought the earth was flat, and
then split into two angry sects because one
division believed the earth to be flatter than the
other. Various other churches exist exclusively
for the purpose of exploring among the forests of
dogma in search of the beast mentioned in Revela-
tions, and every one of these miscellaneous con-
gregations is looking with more or less vigour for
the harassed and badgered heathen with a v iew to
showing him conclusively that it is right and all the
other three hundred are wrong. The unhappy idola-
ter is rapidly being trodden flat by the mad rush of
teachers of all sorts and sizes and descriptions
from all parts of the globe, and, judging from
existing signs, the chances are that, by the time
these assorted apostles have arrived at some com-
mon agreement and are ready to pick up their
victim, and blow the dust off him, and communi-
cate to him even one solid fact that won’t be
contradicted by the next Church that comes along,
the heathen himself will have gone the way the
moa and the Carib went, and will be oxdy a memory
along With the ichthyosaurus and other curiosities
Of the old, dead past.
* #
For these and many other reasons, it might be
advisable to let the unhappy “native” have a
prolonged rest in many countries where he is at
present feeling very tired under the pressure of a
too-aggressive civilisation. The last four centuries
have been a period of feverish activity and excite-
ment, and stupendous efforts have been made in
all quarters of the earth to do something for the
soul of that dark-skinned brother who is alleged
to pine beneath his cocoa-nut palm for want of a
spiritual revelation ; but the results have been so
unsatisfactory that it is evident something is
seriously wrong about the methods employed.
The early Spanish and Portuguese messengers
went forth and judiciously boiled the unbelievers
who came in their way, but possibly they boiled
them too much, for they died out and remained
unconverted to the last. The milder teacher of
the present day uses less violence, but he talks
persistently to his flock, and they wither away
under the influence of his conversation, and
ultimately become extinct. And meanwhile, by
the irony of fate, the so-called heathen is starting
to convert the Christian, and Esoteric Buddhism
promises soon to rank among the fashionable
creeds of the West. This miserably unex-
pected result is the climax of a long series
of disasters, and the enthusiasm concerning
the indefinite tlatiVe is beginning to go slow
in consequence. That deep desire to go out
and do something which, if let alone, would
probably do itself,when the time arrived is one of
the worst features of this era of unrest. The
civilised world has already spent 1890 years in the
effort to fully understand the mysteries of its own
faith, and as the result of these labours it only
partially understands that faith in some 300
different ways, yet whenever it chances to light
upon a stunted, undersized savage musing on ft
lonely shore it seizes Upon him and demands that
he shall Comprehend the whole of it right off, and
draws up elaborate reports from time to time to
show how he is getting on in the attempt. .Some-
times he grows excited under this unwonted pres-
sure, and then a warship comes along and bombards
him in the middle of his perplexities-. Sometimes
he makes a seriqUß effort to master the difficulties
of his heW position, but as a rule something goes
wrong, and he is shot while still engaged among
the rudiments of the faitli which has descended
upon him in, so unlooked-for a fashion. And
under these depressing circumstances it seems
reasonable to suggest that the world should try
tiow the heathen would get on with a little less
active interference —in other words, that, as far as
possible, it should sit down and wait to see what
will happen next. ;
Henry George’s Latest Speech.
Henry George has departed. Both in physical
and mental significance is the phrase exact. The
’Frisco Prophet has abandoned New South Wales,
and he made his formal leave-taking of its people
the occasion for proclaiming his desertion of his
erstwhile devoted and idolatrous followers ahd of
the economic creed to which they had bowed them-
selves down in as abject a mental subjection as
ever a cannibalistic African paid to the paralysing
ugliness of Mumbo-Jumbo. On the night of the
fithinst., Mr, George spoke for the last time in
Sydney, and he signalised the event by a repudia-
tion of the aims, methods and principles of the
local Single-taxers. They had fallen into the
error of supposing that George had meant what
he wrote in “Progress and Poverty”—a Very
natural error, let Us Say—and they accordingly
swore by that book and its author in season and
out of season. To them the book was a veritable
Bible —a sacred Scripture. To them its author
was An Inspired Being—nay, another Messiah, a
second Christ. And now the Being, without the
slightest shade of contrition visible on his counten-
ance, chucks them and their Bible overboard to
drown in a sea of derision. Truly the lot of New
South Wales Single-taxers is hard. Gulled by the
Daily Telegraph, tricked by the Free-trade As-
sociation, flouted by Premier Parkks, and now
repudiated by George himself, their plight would
demand sympathy and compassion were it not
that their now-exploded pretensions had been so
ludicrously inflated. Only a brief two months
before, the “ person”—“ person ” is Parkesian for
a distinguished .Single-taxer—who presided at
George’s farewell meeting, Mr, 0. L. Garland,
M.P., in his annual address to the Single-tax
League, congratulated its members upon
“ the splendidly successful, and supremely
effective efforts made by the talented men
of the League Executive in disseminating the
philosophy of t : e Single-tax upon “the masterly
manner the lea, ne had conducted its great cam-
paign upon “ the fact that the Single-tax party
was the most vigorous political party in New
South Wales;' and upon “its command of the
forces” of disinterested enthusiasm, calmness,
moderation, dignity, and logic. Only two months !
- and, Where is dat barty now? Where are the
other subjects of congratulation ? In the region of
the Gone-Bungs, all. And it is George himself
who has finally sent them there. The Bulletin,
and countless other critics, have been hurrying
them down the slope to the limbo, but the ulti-
mate impulse which propelled them over the verge
came from the Georgian boot.
# * #
No theory eoueerning a proposed social or poli-
tical reform is worth a moment's consideration


from sensible men that is unaccompanied by a
suggestive scheme for its reduction to practice. It
is the idlest nonsense to talk about such-and-such
a plan being good in theory, but bad in practice.
The test of the goodness or badness of any pro-
posal for reform is its capability for use and ad-
justment to the conditions. A tool that won’t
work is-bad, however elegant and finely-finished it
may be itself. The Single-taxers had a dim per-
ception of this truth, and from time to time have
put forth certain notions as to the gradual intro-
duction of the impost. These frequently differed
in detail, but they had one characteristic in
common : they assumed that the incidence of the
tax would ultimately fall upon the accumulated
value of the land, and not merely upon the
increase accruing after a given date—the future
unearned increment. The Single-tax was to be
levied on the whole rental value. To have argued
upon any other basis would have been equivalent
to knocking the Single-tax on the head and to
removing it altogether outside of the sphere of
practical politics. Especially such logical validity
as the Single-taxers could claim for their alliance
wit h the Freetrade party was due to their acceptance
of the position that the appropriation by the State
of the total rental value, less the cost of collection
by the landlords, was the goal of their “ supreme
and splendid ” efforts. Absolute Freetrade, in-
volving the abolition of alb Custom-house taxation
and of all taxation levied in other ways on the
products of labour, was only possible when a
source of revenue was discovered of equal pro-
ductiveness, and this could be found only, accord-
ing to the Single-taxers’ reasoning, in accumulated
laud-values. Nothing less than the total value
could meet the practical condition imposed by the
terms of their hypothesis, for anything less would
fail to yield a revenue commensurate with the
income forfeited by the supersession of the ordi-
nary modes of taxation and with the pecuniary
needs of Government. The very name of the
Single-tax implied all this : otherwise it was a
farcical misnomer.
# # *
According to Henry George, however, his
alleged disciples were absurdly wrong in propos-
ing the absorption of its past and accumulated
value. In that portion of his farewell address
which he devoted to specifically replying to The
Bulletin criticism on his Adelaide speech, he
said -
In our practical proposals we don’t propose to take
from anybody anything that ho already has. We don’t
propose to take "land from the land-owner or unearned
increment from the man who has already got it, but to
tax land values for the future—we will take not the
values of the past, but the values which will accrue in the
future. It will simply put into the public Treasury what
belongs to it, leaving to the individual what belongs to the
individual. Where the unjust ownership of land has en-
abled people to get large properties produced by labour
wj don’t propose to take them for the State. Wo propose
simply to let bygones be bygones, to start fair and as soon
as we can, and in the future to take for the State what
belongs to the community and leave to the individual what
ib longs to him.
If this is the scheme of the New South Wales
Bingle-taxers perfect indeed is the disguise with
which they shrouded it from the public gaze. In
the Australian Standard, the Single-taxers’
■organ, for February, we find the following state-
ments in an article penned by a member of the
■“ talented executive ”
MV have £175,000,000 of taxable (X.S. W. land) value
to replace the loss of revenue from the abolition of all
other taxes. . . . . It must be borne in mind that
Single-taxers propose to make land common property, by
gradually increasing the tax until the whole rental value
rs absorbed.
The author of this a’ticle, which is avowedly an
exposition of the Single tax, as it is headed “A
Practical Chapter,” is W. E. Johnson, and W. E.
Johnson, because of his superior grasp of the
principles of the Single-tax party and the superfine
quality of his organising power, was chosen as the
Secretary t > the “ Henry George Campaign
Committee.” And yet W. E. Johnson, according
to George, didn’t know what Single-taxers pro-
posed. When George, at the conclusion of his
campaign here, asserted that he and his followers
advocated the appropriation by the State of future
value only, he drew the knife across the throat of
his Campaign Secretary and of the Campaign
Committee and thus let out the life from the whole
Single-tax party. That party came into existence
to introduce into our legislative system the pro-
position of “Progress and Poverty ” (book viii.)
to appropriate all rent. Bid future increase in value
is not rent. The unearned increment of the future is
not taxable ; and if the Single-taxers, as Mr. George
asserts, have no intention of confiscating the
values of the past, then Mr. Johnson’s £275,000.000
vanish into the air and the Single-tax and the
Single-tax doctrinaires with them. The moment
George spake the words we quote the Single-tax
organisation of N.S. Wales died, for the reason
of its existence was annihilated, and though its
members may exist as Land Nationalises and
Unearned Incremented, their organism as a dis-
tinct entity, hitherto vital with a definite purpose,
became defunct. And it is their Master and
Founder who dealt them the fatal blow. Et tu,
Brute !
* * *
We have considered the effect of George’s
latest remarks upon the late Georgeites before dis-
cussing their effect upon George himself, because
George has gone, while the corpse of the Single-
taxers remains with vis. But we may not longer
withhold the expression of our opinion that if
Henry George slaughtered his followers he also
committed intellectual suicide. We have re-
marked that the Single-taxers fell into the natural
error of supposing George meant what he wrote
in his famous books. We fell into the same error
ourselves. In this we resembled the Single-taxers,
but we differed from them in declining to accept
George’s views as economically indefeasible. We
have, consequently, been saved from the false
position into which the ’Frisco Prophet drove
himself and his followers when he made the re-
marks above quoted. How miserably false that
position is will be easily perceived by anyone who
will take the trouble to bring our quotation into
contrast with “ Progress and Poverty,” book
vii., chap. iii. “ Why should we hesitate,”
George therein asks, “hi making short work of
such a system (the existing one of land monopoly) ?
Why continue to permit landowners to take the
rent, or compensate them in any manner for the
loss of rent ?” Not a word here as to taxing the
future increment only; he is for dealing decisively
and exhaustively with the accumulations of the
past and the rental value of the present. Nor is
this all—worse, indeed, remains behind. He fol-
lowed up the passage which we have quoted from
his address, by virtually intimating that he had
fallen into line with John Stuart Mill, whose
plan for nationalising the future unearned increase
in the value of land by fixing the present market
value of all lands and appropriating to the State
future increase in value, The Bulletin, in its
issue of March 22, urged Henry George to recon-
sider. George now sees what The Bulletin has
remarked all along—that Mill’s idea is the one
practicable, feasible, scheme possible to Australian
communities. But in his book he denounces it as
inefficient and impracticable, and as possessing
e sential defects. It is conceivable that The
Bulletin’s representations have had some weight
with Mr. George, but, if so, we are not proud of
a convert who at the moment when he perpetrates
a volte-face with respect to a former belief, insists
that he has believed in and preached the newly-
adopted doctrine all the time. Of course an
assertion of this kind amounts to a charge of dis-
ingenuousness, and it is with pain and reluctance
that this journal, which has done more to give
George an honourable Australian reputation than
all other colonial papers put together, levels an
allegation of the kind at the man of whose sin-
cerity and ability it has been ever an ardent
admirer, though it could not accept his economics
without important modifications. The economic
press of the United States for months past has
teemed with similar charges against George.
The Bulletin suspended judgment on these in-
dictments against his mental candour. With the
local evidence now before it, it has no course
before it but to consider George self-deposed from
the pedestal of a philosophic reasoner to the
stump of a political special pleader.
* * *
The Bulletin cordially welcomed George,
though it demurred to the tactical blunder
of which he and his partisans were guilty
in enrolling themselves as greasers to the
wheels of the Freetrade machine. It bids
him good-bye with regret. But let us not be mis-
understood. The feeling with which we part
from the author of “ Progress and Poverty ” has
nothing in common with the sorrow that men feel
when they bid farewell to an honoured guest.
Our regrets are of a different sort —they are of the
description that rise spontaneously in the mind
when a spotless reputation is besmirched or when
an eminent personality grovels in the mud. A
fortnight ago we declined to believe that George,
in materially qualifying by his Adelaide speech
his published notions as to the confiscatory aspect
of his theory, had joined the corps of jugglers
and dodgers with principle. His last Sydney
utterance compelled a change in our opinion. We
are still believers in his logical force —but his skill
in dialectic savours more of the Advocatus Diaboli
than of the patient, truth-seeking, scientific in-
quirer. We still admire his brilliant literary style
—but it renders his ability “ to make the worse
appear the better reason,” the more dangerous.
# # *
George landed in New South Wales with the
fame of a great thinker. He leaves it with a
repute differing only in degree and not in kind
from the discredit that attaches to the pettifogging
attorney, the pliant politician, and the mercenary
clergyman who worships the Almighty dollar
more fervently than the Almighty, He might
have left it honoured as no man ever left its shores
had he risen to the height of the great opportunity
that lay ready to his hand. The very essence of
success as a scientific investigator is the capacity
to receive new light. This he lacked. These
colonies presented to him new conditions and new
data. Were these allowed to modify his precon-
ceived theory ? No ; not till the last moment—
when the evil had been done. Had he declared
for the Mill doctrine at first, he would have
brought Land-Nationalisation within a measure-
able distance. As it is, he has identified it in the
popular mind with robbery—with the confiscation
of the fruits of honest toil as well as of the illegiti-
mate gains of the land-boomer and the speculative
monopolist; and a generation may not suffice to
undo-the mischief. This thing George has done,
and it is the principal result of his mission. There
is small compensation for it in the other result
that he has effectually smashed the Single-taxers
by demonstrating that they have nothing to tax.
The Morality of the Single-Tax.
The Single-taxers have lately developed a keen
anxiety lest they should be suspected of advocating
the principle of confiscation or repudiation. As a
leader in The Bulletin a fortnight ago pointed
out, no student of the Georgian writings can come
to any conclusion, other than that, prior to his
arrival here, Henry George plainly advocated
confiscation. There is, however, nothing very
dreadful in this. Repudiation, confiscation—call
it by what name you choose —though sometimes a
grievous sin, is also at times a high moral action.
There is no need to discuss with our readers the
occasions when repudiation is an evil —these are
obvious enough. As to the converse position—
that repudiation is sometimes a virtue, we have
several modern instances.
* * *
When Arabi, the Egyptian, raised the standard
of Nationalism, it is well understood that he
purposed repudiating the public debt of the
country. To save the money of the bond-
holders and speculators, the British suffocated
the aspirations of the Egyptians, and bombarded
Alexandria. It is true that a certain small portion
of the debt of Egypt was raised for public pur-
poses, but the larger part was, with the knowledge
of the lenders, contracted by the Khedives to
administer to the caprices of themselves and their
courtezans, and for the equipment of soldiery for
the enslavement of the unfortunate people, who
are now burdened witli the interest. Arabi was
not far out in his estimate of the virtue of repu-
diation. A few years ago the New South Wales
Parliament solemnly pledged the country to pay
for ten years a tribute to the British Government
for the maintenance of a fleet for the protection of
British trade. The people will carefully repudiate
the transaction at the first opportunity. The
irresponsible rulers of West Australia have
assigned for insignificant sums vast tracts of
country. These lands have been acquired chiefly
by, or in the interests of, English capitalists, and
will become of enormous value with an expansion
of population. The question will presently arise
whether the acts of irresponsible officials are bind-
ing on the country, should the community require
the land now given away. The answer to this
will be by no means uncertain.
# * *
But, after all, such discussions are futile. There
is a story told of Charles 11., of blessed memory,
that being in a merry humour, he desired the
sages of the Royal Society to consider how it was
that the placing of a live fish in an already fully
water-filled vessel would not result in a displace-
ment of the watery element. The learned pundits
gravely and lengthily discussed the question from
a theoretical point pf view, until the fruitlessness
of argument was shown by the production of a
live fish and a full vessel and an overflowing of
the water consequent on the thrusting of the fish
into the water. The application of this parable
to the Single-taxers is obvious. If their plan will
not work out in practice, it is useless discussing its
theoretic beauties.
# * *
S >me few months ago we pointed out in these
columns that the Jungle-taxers had tailed 10
localise their theories —that it was useless discus-
sing the thing in the abstract, unless it could be
shown that the actual circumstances of the country
admitted of its application. The article served a
useful purpose, as a discussion immediately took
place in the daily papers on this and other points
raised by us. From the discussion we learnt: —
(a) That the Single-taxers do not wish to con-
fiscate rent.
(b) That the purchase-money paid to the State
as well as the cost of all improvements, will be
deducted to ascertain the taxable value of the
land.
(c) That when the rule of the Single- taxers pre-
vails the Customs dues on liquors will not be re-
tained.
This creed is definite, and, taking it for a
basis, it is worth while enquiring what is the
capacity of New South Wales to yield revenue
from unimproved values if all other taxation be
abolished ? First, as to the revenue to be
obtained. By remitting all taxation except on
land-values and stopping the sales of land the
revenue given up would be about £4,000,000
sterling—
Taxation .. .. .. £2,082,000
Land sales .. .. .. 1,212,000
Fees, &c 145,000
£4,039,000
# # #
Single-taxers fix the unimproved value of land
in private hands at 17b millions. From this sum
has to be made certain deductions, viz., payments
to the State on account of purchase of land 28
millions, balances of conditional purchases due to
the State 13 millions—in all, 41 millions. In the
total value of 175 millions are included the clear-
ing, draining and other improvements not in the
nature of erections. To estimate the value of such
improvements would, of course, be now impossible,
but perhaps we may be permitted to assume that,
on the 42 million acres sold, there has been clearing
and improvements of a like nature to the value of
20 millions. This, with the 41 millions stated
above, gives a total of 61 millions to be deducted
from the total of 175 millions, leaving the taxable
value 114 millions. Estimating the rental at
5 per cent., there will be an income of £5,700,000
derived from land eligible for taxation. We have
seen that the income required by the State from
the single-tax would be about four millions, or
70 per cent, of the taxable income.
# #
The present taxes levied by municipalities
amount to about 85 per cent, of the rental value of
land and buildings ; if this tax were to fall on
lands alone, it would bo about 20 per cent, of the
annual value. The system of local government
will probably be extended throughout New South
Wales before the millennium of the Single-tax;
we may therefore assume that the municipal-tax
of 20 per cent, will be in operation when the State
adopts the system, and the landholder will have
to pay this in addition to the 70 percent, taken by
the State. For local and general taxation 90 per
cent, of the rental value of unimproved lands will
thus be hypothecated, leaving for the landowner
10 per cent. This may be meet and proper, but it
looks very like confiscation.
HENRY SIEBEL.
The addleheadedness of Premier Parkes was
never better exemplified than when he made his
condemnatory reference to the “ blunder ' of the
drafters of the American Constitution in adhering
too closely to British precedent, inasmuch as in
the very next breath he pi’oceeded to advo-
cate as the most desirable form of Austra-
lian Federal Government a Privy Council on
the British model, and a double-barrelled Legis-
lature also on the British model. If the
framers of the United States Constitution —“ that
most consummate act of statemanship," as Glad-
stone terms it—erred, it was in their attempt to
assimilate their novel institutions to the essential
spirit of their British prototype. They disre-
garded the form. Parkes, on the other hand,
manifesting once again his incapacity to perform a
solitary deed of constructive statecraft, simply and
slavishly copies the form and the letter and ignores
the spirit. When in the future some misguided
enthusiast establishes on the site of the N.S.W.
National Deadhouse a museum for the multifarious
varieties of colonial and imported cheek, the most
conspicuous specimen in thecrowded Parkes section
should be this political Podsnap’s criticism of the
American patriots of 1787. The American Con-
stitution, faulty though it may be, has stood the
shocks of a fateful century. Parkes’ scheme, as
at present disclosed, is already riddled. He has
patched it up since he first revealed it, and it will
need re-patching till it is in the condition of
Paddy Hooligan’s coat, in which the only
portion of the original garment retained was the
arm-holes.
“ Ecce Homo,” a pamphlet in which Mr. P. .1.
Gandon, a person of Imperialistic proclivities,
endeavours to bring together “ the various objec-
tions to Henry George’s views urged by the
ablest of his opponents ” is before us. The writer’s
ability to deal with the subject may be gauged
from the fact that he asserts that George’s
“ rooted and republican prejudice against what he
(George) calls ‘ trumpery titles ’ is not worthy of
him, since the titles of at least the British nobility
and aristocracy represent not only theoretically,
but in the majority of cases actually the honour
which the people pay through their accre-
dited head to talents, bravery, worth, or
valued service.” Greater nonsense than this
was never penned —■ its untruthfulness need
scarcely be pointed out. The Bulletin, however,
would like to know how many among the aris-
tocracy selected by Mr. Gandon as being especially
entitled to honour have earned their titles through
the possession of “ talents, bravery, or worth,” or
by “ valued services ” We think there are hardly
a dozen British peers who have personally earned
anything by the display of any good quality or
in the performance of any public duty. As for
those who have inherited titles, which were
bestowed on their ancestors, not by the people, but
by “their accredited heads,” for the purposes of
the said heads, inmost cases vile, what of them?
As a rule, it can be only said that the present
holders of titles are by no means digraced by their
relationship to the courtesans and “favourites”
from whose loins they spring and who were
ennobled because their “ valued services ” pleased
the filthy palates of some jaded voluptuary. After
this we may safely leave the remainder of the
writer’s arguments to be dealt with by the Single-
taxers. Those who would learn the shameful
story of the genesis of the British nobility can do
so for a shilling—the price of Howard Evans’
book, “ Our Old Nobility.”

A late number of the Universal Review con
tains a lengthy descriptive review of Tolstoi’s
latest work, the “ Kreutzer Sonata,” a tale of
married unhappiness and its causes, not yet pub-
lished in Russian owing to Press censorship and
now being translated into English from the talented
and eccentric author’s manuscript by Miss Hap-
good. The hero, a “psychopath,” throws a
libertine in his wife’s way who is musical, watches
them during the playing of Beethoven's lovely
sonata, then leaves home and, returning to find
them together, kills his wife, and is acquitted.
The plain speaking of the book will cause a great
sensation in prudish British homes. For instance,
the author points out that a union brought about
merely by physical qualifications, and their taste-
and-try-before-you-buy revelation in low-necked
dresses or short skirts, cannot fail to be unhappy;
The “lust of the eye” is unquestionably a most
unstable foundation for the temple to Juno, and a
similarity of tastes and intellectual interests the
only one calculated to bear an edifice that will
stand the test of time
“ An Officer’s Wife ” writes to the press concern-
ing the hom-s during which the officers of the
Australian intercolonial boats labour :
Witness the hours of continuous labour in port, Cargo
is being constantly taken in there, and what chance has
this much-worked man of getting to his home ? Perhaps
the vessel arrives at 1 o’clock in the morning at the
wharf, it is fully 2 a.m. before he can leave the steamer,
worn out with calls on every hand— 3, most probably,
before he reaches home, and another hour will have
nearly gone before his wet clothes are off and he can lie
down for a snatch of sleep. He must be up again at half-
past otobe on hoard by “turn-to” time. So he has
had two hours and a-half for rest. Back he goes to the
cargo, with no further chance of seeing his home, and
after another 14 or 15 hours on deck, away to sea and his
watch on deck. Little the passengers sleeping below
dream that their very lives are in the keeping of this
underpaid, overworked official, whom everything tends
to overpower with sleep before he leaves. And for all
this what is his pay? If second officer, at £1 monthly,
perhaps, more than the sailor ; and if chief, £4 more, and
this pay is for constant attendance. He has no time for
home at all. Other men in almost any branch of
life have a little while to see their families; these
men literally none. On deck they must he looking
after cargo. When the engine-room fires are out,
engineers can leave, yet their pay is far higher than
the deck-officer who remains at work all day. And they
must be men who hold certificates for their seamanship,
too. Many of them have been captains, and so passed all
the grades of efficiency to the highest, and they are taken
in by the companies and worked like omnibus-horses. It
is a mere question of time when a worn-out officer falls
out of his working life. What is done for them then ?
Nothing. I could write more. I could tell you of the bad
effect on his moral life this want of all home influence
tends to. I could speak of the want of interest in wife
and children it naturally engenders.
The remedy ? It lies with the labour unions.
Premier Parkes’ speech, in moving the Conven-
tion resolutions in the New South Wales Assembly,
demonstrated the chaotic shapelessness of his con-
ception of Federation responsibilities. The soli-
tary illustration —that of the Broken Hill lunatic
—which he furnished of the way in which the
Federal Executive would work, was ludicrously
absurd. According to Premier Parkes, the
Federal Government would assume control of the
crazed person and place him in an asylum
at Adelaide, while under the present colonial
system the bereft wretch would be com-
pelled to make the journey to Sydney. This
illustration, if it has any cogency at all,
means that the care of lunatics, the charge of
criminals, and the regulation of police, will be
handed over to the Federal Government, and when
these purely executive functions are transferred,
others must necessarily follow till finally the pro-
vincial governments and legislatures will be bored
like the unemployed, and “ have no work to
do-o.” If this is the sort of thing that Parkes
means by Federation, the people of the colonies
had better understand clearly that federation on
Parkes’ lines involves the suppression of their
individual Parliaments and the institution of a
centralised autocracy. From one standpoint it
wouldn’t matter very much if they were annihi-
lated, but it is just as well colonists should know
how it is proposed to affect their institutions.
And, with particular respect to lunatic-asylums,
it should be said that each of the American
States and Canadian provinces supports its own
public asylum from provincial funds. Parkes
should stick to vague bombast and bogus genera-
lisations. When he tries to elucidate detail he
slops over.
■ ♦ ;
Again has the futile agitation against the Vic-
torian Education Act been revived, the Presby-
terian Assembly rushing once more into the
breach last week, led by Dr. Harper, with a long
string of propositions, wherein the falsehoods
began in the very first, to the effect that there was
no intention to revive denominationalisrn. Higin-
botham offered the only possible compromise by
his Education Bill twenty years ago, and when the
pestiferous sects overthrew that, nought remained
but the purely secular. The foolish idea of Harper
and Co. now is to introduce the thin end of the
wedge by making the ordinary school-teachers
read the Bible to their classes. It is to work
like a Mumbo-Jumbo fetich, notwithstanding that
two-thirds of the teachers don’t believe in it.
The solemn fact of the matter is simply this ; the
Victorian sects combined to pass the Education
Act with a view, as Minister Stephen bluntly but
unguardedly put it, of dealing a death-blow at
R.C.-dom. They now find that they have con-
solidated the Pope’s influence, and have helped to
wipe out their own religion—a clear and beautiful
illustration of the folly of cutting off your nose to
spite your face. The Pope and Co. have a policy
and adhere to it day and night, in shade and
shine ; the educational policy of the other set has
up to now merely been to hate the Roman
Catholics, and, because of that hate, to lend all
possible aid to the blotting out of even the name
of God from the State-school books. The
Agnostic looks on and laughs. If the whole of
the sects could agree, the world would be in chains
for another hundred years.

The newspapers characterise as “liberal” the
judgment of the New South Wales Full Court in
regard to the case of M‘Laughlin v. the Even-
ing News, and almost every writer who has com-
mented on the question has said something or
other about the “ privileges ”of the Press. As a
matter of fact, the Press legally has, and ought to
have, no special privileges whatsoever. In 9l>
cases out of a hundred the Press is a purely com-
mercial concern, and in proof of this you have only
to be behind the scenes for a week or two. The
Press, we repeat, has no special rights, and should
have none, because anybody with a pound or two
can set up a press and call himself “ we.’' But
then, it may be urged, all papers are not alike—
look at the fine, old, dignified, stately, repectable
Tory daily papers, like the Times and the
Argus and the S.M. Herald ! Well, look at the
them —what are they ? Simply the organs of a set
of people more or less ignorant and purse-proud
and pompous, who have managed to conserve a
commercial monopoly which came by inheritance
into their hands. The fact of the matter is that
all the Press can claim is what every citizen down
to the lowest bummer on the side-walk should be
able to claim —the right of free thought and of
free speech right up to the line of a fair libel law.
When the press is arrogant enough to talk about
its “privileges,” meaning thereby something
which is not possessed by .1. Smith, the barber's
deputy, or T. Jones, the humble person who
comes at night to remove dead cows from your
promises for a trilling fee, it is preparing an
engine for its own destruction at the hands of a
people who wish no more to hi press ridden than
they do to be priest-ridden.

It speaks libraries for Mrs. Potter’s artistic success that
in “ Camille” (the dilettante pronounce it Kaui-ee),

only the poor, weak-chested badgered Delilah whose sub*

conceived him) portrays a noble feminine attribute su.*
prisingly general—behind the footlights. Between
throbs, however, your Sappho comprehended that the
drapery was very dazzling, especially the gown worn in
the second act, which all the women in front must have
envied till their consciences pricked them. But, to do
things in order, let me commence with Frock first, which
came with a shock to people with fixed “ you may trim
yellow with black ” ideas and who vote an artist “ scenic”
if he robes a red-haired girl in sage-gseen. The gown was
in sunflower-yellow satin en Princesse, with thick ruche
round the hem of yellow ostrich feathers, classic over-
dress, caught on the shoulder and again at the waist, in
dragon-green jetted Russian-net with a deep border
design in jet ; the full sleeves to the elbow and folds
about the corsage of yellow silk gauze ; Roman net of
green jet, pink camellias (of course) and spray of stage-
diamonds in the hair, yellow shoes, a gorgeous peacock-
feather, and diamond girdle forming the necessary
etceteras, Tnis was bright and daring and pleasing on
the whole, but the second gown was a cut-bias eye-opener
for local fashion which considers itself en grande toilette
when it s gotten up in cot ton-back satin and a pearl bead.
Poetically it suggested a pale-grey sky dashed with
the pink and silver of sunset, and it seemed almost
desecration to appraise such a vision in the jargon of
the warehouse, to detail that the jupe was in faint
amaranth pink satin, draped with* white Mechlin lace
sprinkled with silver, the corsage also of the pink, cut
square and edged frontally with a deep lace frill, with
Zouave in pale-grey satin figured in large floral design in
paler grey, which material was repeated in the one-breadth
snakey train coming from the left shoulder, where it was
pleated d la Medici with touches of pink, and caught
bunchily at the waist with another suggestion of the
contrast, the Directoire skirt panels in plain pale grey
with three wide borderings in glistening silver, while the
sleeves, reaching with the lace frill almost to the finger-
tips, were in figured grey satin with puffed shoulders and
a jetted lattice which covered the entire arm. The crown-
less hat was in drawn gauze with pink plumes. The cottage
gown was simple, of course (Kam-ee has shunted diamonds
for devotion), but, then, the hang was heavenly. It was
in lilac cashmere the overskirt, bordered round the feet
and up the batik with creamy wide lace laid on flat, worn
over a silk underskirt. The bodice, fastening up the
back, draped d la Zouave, with cream piece lace loosely
gathered in the front ; the sleeves—full and ridged, with
underpart which dodgily became upper part at the wrist
—of cream lace ; long spring-green sash and a rustic
“shape.” The next gown—the Mte costume—was rather
meretricious in motive; the train was in eau-de-nil silk,
with jupe shrouded in vivid cabbage-green gauze edges
at the feet, with silver bullion and a deep design in silver,
while down the side fall white camellia buds and leaves.
The bodice was looped across with strings of pearls, the
hanging shoulder-wings were in myriad glistening silver
strands, while show-paste radiated everywhere and the
floats looked dim and sickly. By the way, the Potterian
mode of making-up the eyes is strongly reminiscent of
Our Nellie.
Probably to scare the silver-fish out of Mrs. Potter's
Pauline wardrobe the “ Lady of Lyons” was sumptuously
staged at Sydney Royal, on 10th inst., since beyond the
dazzle of divine drapery nothing much remains to be
mentioned. In the second act, where she first makes en-
trance, Mrs. Potter wears an Empire gown (as usual), the
front of cream-satin draped in spangled gauze with a
whirl of silver round the feet; the long train, in emerald
green velvet with edge pattern in large silver leaves
coming from between the shoulder-blades and appearing
under the arm also ; a narrow hand of green velvet accen-
tuating the high waist and two narrow hands coming
brace-like over the shoulders. The sleeves (in cream)
were puffed and slashed with silver at the top and were
fulled to below the elbow with a line of silver down the
inside seam which was indescribably effective during the
hugging intervals ; two bows of cream ribbon edged with
silver fringe on the right side of the train, which had an
under trainlet of cream-satin so that the Potterian ankles
should remain undiscovered, no matter how frantic the
stage directions became. The neck was cut round and
edged with soft fulled cream-tulle, two windmill ridges of
wired white lace standing up on either shoulder ; finishing
touches being white silk mittens, a big bouquet, a
diamond parure, and green velvet slippers. In act three
the costume is a gull-grey poplin with deep kilting in grey
round the feet and cable design in brown fur above it,
made all in one (Mrs. P. never affects a jacket bodice), but
dodgily designed above the waist to simulate a sort of
Figaro jacket in grey heliotrope velvet with the points
turned back in front, and lined with grey over folds of
the poplin ; the hack draperies pleated high on the waist
and topped with th j velvet, of which material the long,
ridged sleeves arc made—with a line of grey down the
inside seam. An effective relief to the Noah's Ark
plainness of tb Princesse front was the outlining of the
gore seams in a narrow, brown line. The neck was cut
square and filled in with white muslin, with collar of real
lace, a brown fur boa and an Empire bonnet in brow n and
grey being worn at first. Mrs. Potter also wore grey
satin shoes and a turquoise and diamond brooch. In act
fourthe gowning is t lie same, of course (Pauline, to all
appearances, being one of those rare brides who embark
on a honeymoon with no other impedimenta than a hand-
bag), but in the last act Mrs. Potter being in mauve satin
draped in black gauze designed on the upper part in three
yertioftl lines of large silver stars, below them two
•wide scallops of black jet, below that again an in-
tricate bordering of silver stars and black jet—the waist
'defined by a girdle of silver and black. The overdress,
'with long, fulled sleeves and voluminous train, in black
[Velvet with large brocade pattern in black silk. Mauve
Shoes, the presence of two crimson roses in the hair light-
ing up the gown, perhaps, but mitigating the “sorrow” of
it. At the end of the 4th act Mrs. Potter was the recipient
Of much green-grocery—one lot taking the form of a large
floral ship in full rig, which would have been very nice if
only it hadn’t looked as though intended to convey a
large, cruel hint to the willowy artiste.
To Hatter the amour propre of Sydney society your
Sappho boldly asserts that the rain alone was responsible
for the gorgeous total of (comparatively) three men and a
boy under V.R. patronage who formed the audience at
Miss Menk-Meyer’s first recital at Sydney Y.M.C.A. on 6th
inst. A mere handful of humanity turned up attired in
straying back-hair and wool clouds alternating with last
season’s hats and wet umbrellas. Miss Menk-Meyer—who
is slight and very girlish, so much so that in Paris admir-
ing hearers contracted the habit of sending her balls and
toys to amuse herself with—played with marvellous
facility several classic “ morso ” of the most howling
school at which the cognoscenti present turned up their
eyes like ducks in wet weather and tried to look like a
cat does when you stroke down towards her tail. Setting
technique aside, it was only natural the pianiste should
be at her sympathetic best in her own compositions, the
prettiest of those performed being, in my opinion, the
“Berceuse” (by the way, somebody once objected to Miss
Menk-Meyer that the only fault to be found with this was
that it was “too sleepy” I) and the “Danse Phan-
tome,” a weird thing with a rattle of dead bones
in it, which occurs in her opera, “ Herman Doro-
thea.” Lord and Lady Carington, who arrived at
half-time, apparently enjoyed the performance to a
degree, her ladyship, after it was over, sending Miss
Menk-Meyer congratulatory messages per Charlie Huener-
bein. As to the tracking—the vice-regal garment was in
mauve-pink striped silk with semi-Medici collar and front
of white lace—Watteau fan with mother-o’-pearl sticks
and a mauve-pink bow and aigrette held by a diamond
cluster in the hair-cream satin and lace-oaped opera-
cloak. By the way, Lady Carington’s habit of carrying
her train in her hand up and down all passages, aisles,
etc., shows how her gathers have suffered at the large,
wide feet of the Government House attache, doesn’t it ?
Miss Menk-Meyer wore a Worth-built confection. The
Directoire bodice cut square and very low in front, was in
cream striped satin falling on to the skirt in three long
panels, the centre one simulating a sash, over a cream
tulle skirt with front composed of tiny frills to the waist
—front of the bodice in plain satin, butterfly shoulder-
bows in white ribbons, and a few of those naughty-looking
diamonds studded on the corsage. Although the audience
was small it was enthusiastic, Miss Menk-Meyer being-
recalled several times, and receiving a bouquet and a
basket of flowers across the floats, while several admirers
sent vegetables behind in a strange desire to do “dark”
homage, seemingly.
On the afternoon following her first recital, Miss Menk-
Meyer, who is morally -sustained on tour by her elder
sister, held a reception at the Grosvenor which was well
attendee!, though, owing to a reporter’s mistake, several
called the previous day and were unable to see the object
of their visit. In private, Miss Menk-Meyer is an un-
affected girlish girl—a necessary distinction in a genera-
tion of sour-mannered damsels with strong opinions on
the suffrage question. She wore a cream gown edged
with silvered galon and held at the waist by a gold and
silver tinsel girdle, and talked with a simplicity which was
like water to a thirsty soul tired of the verbal posings of
the average prodigy. After the Sydney season she pro-
poses an American tour, and after that to Vienna to see
her opera produced. “It can only meet the same fate as
‘ Don Giovanni,’ ” she said quietly—" it can only be hissed
from one end of Europe to the other.”
The second Menk-Meyer'recital on Bth instant was, of
course, carefully shunned by local culchaw which really
cannot screw itself up to classical point unless harnessed
on to a subscription-list, when it attends like clockwork
to get its money’s worth. But that the two or three
which gathered together had brought their souls along
was evidenced by one fair damsel, who, as Miss Menk-
Meyer evolved the solemn largo movement from the
“Sonata Pathetique,” dropped a damp tributory tear
which I was glad to see didn’t bring away any bloom of
beauty with it. I was going to bottle that moisture and
send it to the artiste, only I reflected that she probably
had a cellar-full already. By the way, people were sur-
prised to see that the bill comprised selections from ten
composers, after the preliminary announcement that each
evening would be devoted to one at a time. In this the
pianiste yielded to the local tipsters, who advised a more
“varied programme.” Why, certainly, but they should
have squeezed in a comic song or a pas de quatre since it’s
so easy to “knock ’em,” as the slangy actor declares.
Put a girl in tights that quite “shock” them, and you’ll
want S.R.O. on the stairs.
The Caringtonian “ At Home ” on Bth inst. was more
generally dedicated to the canvas-backed ducks of local
society than is commonly the case on these occasions,
though I have remarked that unfashionable vulgarity is
always more timid of attending an “At Home” than a
“reception,” which, to their intelligence, has a wider and,
if I may coin a word, mob-ier significance. ’Twas a
bewchus day—the weather ever comes to vice-regal
aid—and your Sappho spied one or two satisfactory gown-
ings. Lady Carington appeared in grey woollen polonaise
cut out round the waist in turrets with guipure braid
which outlined the straight skirt side-panels worn over a
jupe of fawnish velvet; folded front, with frill of rose-pink
chiffon ; grey straw bonnet trimmed with pink roses, grey
velvet, and fawn wings ; spotted tulle veil, with gloves ;
the three little Caringtons wore terra-cotta ulsters
and cloth-gathered hats in the same shade. Mrs.
Burdekin, fawn woollen, with a tiny brown flower draped
over a jupe of seal-brown velvet, ridged sleeves of the
velvet and braidings of fawn guipure, small brown bonnet
with pointy wings and autumn leaves. Miss Florence
Menk-Meyer (to whom Lord and Lady Carington were
especially attentive) came with her brother, Dr. Felix
Meyer, and wore a cream skirt, with Russian cap and cape
of beautiful black fur. Mrs. Carruthers was charming in
pale-grey tweed with deep band of grey fur round the
skirt, fur outlining the bodice, and a wide white felt hat
with ostrich feathers. Then a statuesque, lovely woman
wore a mingling of deep brown and chamois leather, with
a brown feather in her bonnet. An excited damsel car-
ried a piece of cake around as if it were a card-case; and
brother Rupert was vivid in a tartan T»m Thumb tic.
The third day of the Lawn Tennis Tournament (10th
inst.) drew a crowd of the very nicest people to Sydney
Association Ground to w itness the battle for the Cooper
Champion Cup, which is a big, honest-looking tankard
large enough to asphyxiate a pup in, and the other
splendid trophies, ranging down to a silver pencil-case,
which were displayed in a marquee on the lawn. Lord
and Lady Carington, with Lud\ Scott and Captain Del-
bert, rode over during the afternoon ; the vice-regal rig-
out of short block cloth habit, deep white collar, black
hard felt hat, and black net-veil, with white gloves and
hunting crop, appearing far more neat and workmanlike
than Lady Scott's dowdy-looking black skirt, loose fawn
jacket and sailor hat with red-and-blue-band. On the
ground thedressing was very good—most of the frocks but
toning up the back—a very pretty model being in electric-
grey cloth with three capes and capote of souvenir roses ;
another satisfactory gown was a back-fastened chrome-
green, with ridged sleeves of green velvet, green gloves,
and green velvet bonnet with yellow roses. Miss Roberts
wore a charming gown in Assyrian red, trimmed with
satin and velvet-stripped trimming, capote of nasturtium
flowers and shaded velvet leaves, silk parasol en suite.
Miss Garran wore dark-blue serge, with carnation red silk
front and guipure braiding. A taking toilette was in
black and grey lizard-striped silk, with folded front of
white chiffon, Alsatian bow of true red velvet adorned
with black jet on the hair. It was a pity there wasn’t a
band to give a snap to the promenading, which wag
greatly indulged in, for tennis is a very sad spectacle
except to the enthusiasts of the racket. I was glad to see
that the exorbitant admission rate of three-and-sixpence
charged last year has come down to half-a-crown this
time.

“ Camille,” the impossible story of a con-
sumptive cocotte, her paramours and parasites,
caught on at once at Sydney Royal, because botli
men and women revel in that kind of thing,
because of the magnificence of Mrs, Potter’s
various gowns, because of the excellence of the
acting all round, and, lastly, because of tha
manner in which it was staged. Cordon’s interiors
(they did not quite fit the stage) were very fins
samples of the delicate French style of internal
decoration, and were not —it occasionally happens
at this house —killed by being allied to gorgeous
and gaudy upholstery. As the self-sacrificing
(’amille, Mrs. Potter was undeniably good, despite
her tragic gait and angularity of gesture. Her
maimer of taking calls in the middle of an act
must be taken exception to. The manner of her
exit in the third act, when she tears herself away
from Armand,- called forth some well-deserved
applause, whereupon Mrs. P. poked her head
in at the O.P. door and bowed in acknow-
ledgment and it looked very much as if
another round would have evoked the repetition
of her last line and exit. Kyrle Bellew’s Armand
I Rival was also a very good performance, but his
peacocky walk palls somewhat on the male palate.
As Varville, Edward Sass takes full advantage of
his only good opportunity at the close of the fourth
act. Recognising that his acting conduced greatly
to the scene’s success, the “gods” determined to
have him before the curtain, and did, albeit he
came in the triumphant train of Mrs. Potter. His
delay was the sole reason for Mrs. Potter’s
numerous alleged calls at the end of the fourth
act. Emma Gywnne, asNichette, caught the pub-
lic fancy on this her first Sydney appearance.
Everyone was glad to see Mrs. Edwin Bryer
once more. She would have proved useful also in
the “ Lady of Lyons.” The Potter-Bellew sea-
son has been extended three weeks, and “ Camille”
will be reproduced on Saturday.
$£
The “ Lady of Lyons,” that miserable story
of despicable prevarication and un.scrupulousness
which the wordy ocean of grandiloquent bosh on
whose bosom it is borne cannot dignify, drew
good houses during the three nights of its repre-
sentation, chiefly because it is fashionable to go
to the Royal just now. The scenery was made up
chiefly by resurrections, but the avenue scene was
a fine specimen of Gordon’s work, though probably
Mr. \ aughan, the local artist, has something to do
with its success. The Pauline of our dreams was not
materialised in Mrs. Potter, whose wardrobe is not
yet exhausted, and whose lines were delivered in a
series of jerks emphasised by ungraceful spasmodic
tosses of the arm. As Claude Melnotte, Kyrle
Bellew possessed outwardly all the manly attri-
butes that could win a woman’s heart or retain
her affection, but his rendering, not the traditional
one, only confirmed our opinion that Melnotte is a
creature whom any ordinary man would like to
kick. Wilson Forbes was usually supportable as
Colonel Damas ; all the other people were bad as
they make ’em.

After a highly successful six weeks’ season
“ Now-a-JJays,” at Her Majesty’s (Sydney), will
give place to the “Two Orphans” on Saturday
u’ght, when William Rignold will assume his
“big” part of Jacques, in which he made over
2000 appearances in London.
** * *
“ Fun on the Bristol ” is put on at Sydney
Criterion in a manner which completely eclipses
all prior representations. In addition to the gay
O’Brien interior and the gorgeous saloon scene on
the Bristol, a third act, admirably staged, show-
ing the vessel’s deck (when the fun is fast and
furious), has been tagged on. John E. Sheridan,
wishful apparently to outdo his fair country-
woman in the gowning business, as the ignorant,
shrewd, good-hearted hot-tempered Widow
O’Brien, is better than ever, and his sketches of
the drunken dude, filthy drab and old tramp,
with songs, “ Razzle-dazzle ” and a wittily coarse
parody on “ Queen of My Heart,” are excellent
pieces of “ make-up ” and character-acting. The
support is very good indeed. In Grade Whiteford
and Lena Salinger the management have two
gems of the first Water, and the interpolated
variety business alone is good enough to run the
show.
* * * *-
“ Mamma ” was revived at the Gaiety on
Saturday night, with much success. As Pontifex,
the musical plagiarist, Frank Thornton, not per-
haps so well-placed as previously, is very amusing.
Messrs. Brown, Cates and Corlesse are equal to
every emergency. As Mrs. Janaway, the relent-
less mother-in-law, Annie Taylor is in a part
which fits her, and Diana and Winifred are
pleasingly interpreted by Ada Lee and Brenda
Gibson. The curtain is raised with “My
Milliner’s Bill,” in which Frank Cates and Emma
Bronton scintillate.

The Maccabe season, at Sydney School of
Arts, grows nightly in popularity, and the veteran
monologuist, whose name we inadvertently
omitted from the list of those who performed for
the benefit of the Bourke Flood fund, is apparently
as great a favourite as ever. Manager Buller in-
forms us that matinee performances will he given
on Saturdays in future, when children will be
admitted to all parts of the house on payment of
sixpence.

At Bondi Aquarium the U.jS. Minstrels,
with whom are associated Billy Warner, Florence
Schuler, and Amy Rowe, are drawing good
audiences. Last Saturday the children of the
Secular Lyceum sang two numbers with a har-
monious freshness that delighted everyone. The
club’s whisky is excellent.

Darlinghurst Skating Rink, usually sup-
posed to lie the largest in the world, continues to
get comfortably filled on every week night with
busy skaters and their admiring friends. The
retiring-rooms are numerous and well-appointed.
