“ After Dark ” fetched a vast multitude to
Melbourne Royal, on Saturday, the house being
absolutely packed for the first time during the
current season. The plot of this lively old night-
mare is worked out with more than the usual
amount of infidelity to nature, and the characters
are far less human than a stuffed Guy Fawkes, but,
take it altogether, “After Dark” is a riot of
absorbing interest for the scene-shifters, likewise a
rousing source of anxiety to the stage-manager,
and a misguided triumph for artist Brunton. The
business is carried on mainly by a brigade of cold-
blooded villains under dark arches, and in gamb-
ling hells, railway stations, and back garrets.
Virtue is represented by three born idiots, the
chief of whom, having reduced himself to a moral
and physical wreck with two penn’orths of gin, is
told off to pull the others from a watery grave, or
remove them off the track of a furious cardboard
train. There is no kind of sense about any of
these good people, and they seem to live for no
better purpose than to be dropped down a hole in
the floor to the strains of “ We wont go home
till morning.” When “After Dark” was
first produced it contained a music-hall quite
independent of the gambling den, but in
the present instance these two scenes ore mixed
up whereby the poetry of the conception is
weakened and impaired. Otherwise matters are
even more realistic than of yore. The underground
railway scene is effective, and the Royal tank does
duty for the Thames, into which a couple of supers,
make actual cold plunges, under pretence of being
Charles Warner and his daughter. This amiable
delusion was treated with great consideration by
the gallery-boys, who mercifully refrained from
fetching Charles Warner and Gracie before the
curtain, so that their dryness and dustiness might
not be exposed to a scoffing world. Of the acting
it is impossible to speak in terms of too little
praise. Charles Warner, as the drunken “Old
Tom,” holds the mirror up to his own everlasting
self and reflects his rather wearisome image. The
others, having nothing that is compatible with
possibility to do, are merely to be commiserated
on the fact that “ After Dark” is quite likely to
run for three weeks, by the grace of a “ puffing
billy ” and a cold hath.

“ Uncle Dick’s Darling,” at the Princess’,
was produced before an audience who expressed
their highly qualified approval in limited num-
bers. Considering that Byron’s drama is about
the same as new to Melbourne, the public indis-
position to roll up and see what it is like can only
he regarded as an awful symptom of Mr. J. L.
Toole’s decline, or, as is more certain, of a grow-
ing irreverence for his company. “Uncle Dick’s
Darling” made hut a moderate success at the
Gaiety, more than twenty years ago, when the
comedian was surrounded by people like Irving,
Adelaide Neilson, John Clayton, Mrs. Henry
Leigh, and others, suited to the work. Any com-
parison between the performances of then and
now would be too cruel. Toole himself is rather
less lively and a fraction more pathetic than of
yore, and the rest of the show may be summed up
as inferior to that given under Phil Day at the
Alexandra a while back. In particular, Billing-
lington’s performance, of Mr. Chevenix can be
cited as a ridiculous perversion of the author’s
meaning. This unfortunate fizzle is supplemented
by “Off the Line.” The fact that such a little
gem is being continued for a second week may
assist matters somewhat. Next Saturday they
revive “ A Serious Family.”
416 4(6 4(6
The Alexandra is at present in the throes
of “Marvellous Melbourne,” a work containing
the most agony at the price of any of Mr. Dam-
pier’s florid successes. It is a collection of shudders
relieved by whoops of merriment, and the local
colour is laid on two feet deep. Here they drown
themselves in the Yarra instead of in the Thames,
and rather than go 12 000 miles for a Waterloo
bridge they fix up a distant view of the last new
structure that spans the Marvellous City’s own
particular ditch. The interpretation of this
attractive outrage is decidedly better than it used
to be, even Boothman being guilty of a commend-
able disposition to move with the times. But as
the Dampier season draws nigh to its close a
further change to Marcus Clarke’s “ Term of His
Natural Life ” will take place on Saturday next.

“ Paul Jones ” is another matter calling
for nothing but honourable mention of its con-
stant success. Among a round dozen of reasons
why this opera promises to heat the record the
least obtrusive is a heavy father into whom Hodson
infuses about six times the usual quantity of
humour found in such trying characters. An
eccentric duet between Nellie Stewart and this
artistic old geezer is one of the trickiest things in
a specially cunning entertainment.

Toole called upon the gallant Captain
de Burgh to explain the meaning of his having
produced “ Uncle Dick’s Darling' at Melbourne
Alexandra without consulting J.L.T. in the
matter. The Capting's story was to the effect that
poor, dead Phil. Day professed to have bought the
Australian rights of “ Uncle [lick's Darling, and
was consequently the sole offender in a transac-
tion which turned out unprofitable to all parties
concerned. This drama has been Toole's very own
from the first. He specially commissioned H. .1,
Byron to write something to suit him, hut, the
“ Darling ” has never seemed to suit anybody par-
ticularly well.

That medley of distorted ideas and im-
probable situations, represented by some half a-
hundred boisterous characters, and christened
by the name of “ New Babylon, ’ has been
crowding the popular portions ot- the Adelaide
Royal for a week. This satisfactory business can
not. he on account of the piece itself, but must he
ascribed to the realistic manner in which it is
staged by Bland Holt, aided by an able interpre-
tation by the members of his company. Bland
Holt’s Flotsam is as good as ever, and H. Sea-
grave, Gus, Glover, Mrs Holt, and Miss Maggie
Knight are the most conspicuous of the others.

The Van Tassells have caused some excite-
ment in Adelaide apart from their balloon-asoent
business. It appears that Mr. Van Tassell and
Madame Cora entered into partnership in Mel-
bourne, and half the proceeds of the balloon
shows were to go to each. . Tassell went to
Adelaide to arrange ascensions, met Edwin
Thorne, the actor, and promised him “whacks.”
Madame Cora, after the first exhibition in
Adelaide, claimed her share of the takings, but
both Tassell and Thorne objected, whereupon the
gay Tassell was arrested, and did not get clear of
the infuriated Madame, or rather the officers of
the law whom she had set at work, till he had
parted with £BO. That is one side of the story,
anyhow.

Madame Ristori’s “ Autobiography” is to
hand in Australia, and will be found capital read-
ing lor an hour or two. She never travelled the
royal road of a Langtry or Potter, but had to go all
through the mill, associated with actors like Sal-
'ini, Rossi, and Majcrones brother. Very in-
teresting is the account of her rivalry with Rachel
in Paris, but the pluckiest thing of her life was
learning the English language, with which she
wound up her English and American tours. Her
reminiscences touch pleasantly on Sydney and Mel-
bourne, with special reminiscences of the Sydney
people, though she forgets to mention how clum-
sily Gatehouse, that funny Mayor, lifted the
golden wreath on her head at the Melbourne Town
Hall, so that it lodged over one eye. Good old
Gatehouse ! He’s still extant, but rides home-
ward no more on saveloy-machines while the Town
Hall clock booms midnight.

Editor Bulletin’,— In your issue of March 20, I find a
par. wherein the writer quotes (and, I am sorry to add,
misquotes) an article of mine in a New Zealand paper on
accent. The misquoting evidently arises from tlie
necessity of brevity, and in no way affects the point he
ra ses. Permit me to say that in stating that
the word accent is misleading, and that the word
should be tune , I was in a difficulty to find
a better word. 1 want one which will represent some-
thing between tone and tune, but the word I used is
the best which the exigencies of terminology will admit of.
When he sajs that what I have written on the subject
“ is unintelligible, most of it," 1 accept his dictum and say
it may be, In him. The question with which he seeks to
pose me about an Irish song, sung by men of three
different nationalities, is reminiscent of a very old
story, what he would call a chestnut. In Spof-
forth’s glee, “Hail Smiling Morn,” there is a passage in
which the words ' ‘ flies away ” are repeated by the sepa-
rate voices and an Irishman and a Scotchman are
the members of the glee party. After the Englishman
and Irishman have sung “ Hies away,” the Scotchman
sings “flees awa." I think your critic has confounded
accent—or what, for want of a better word, I call tune—
with pronunciation 1 hope he will deem this explana-
tion intelligible.—Yours, &c,. Fred. Maccask. [lf the
Irishman and Englishman pronounce the words “flies
away ” in similar fashion, sing them in the same time, to
the same tune, what better term than “accent” can be
found to define the something which makes their different
nationality apparent ?— Ed. B.]
The only photograph of Henrik Ibsen in Australia is in
the window of Allan’s music shop, Collins-street, and is
the large one presented by Ibsen to Janet Achurch, in
commemoration of her acting as Nora, in “A Doll’s
House,” at the Novelty Theatre, London. Underneath is
Ibsen’s own sprawling and delighted autograph, of large
size.

The last production of “ La Tosca,” at Sydney Royal,
was disgraced by the antics and bowlings of a number of
University hoodlums—who did what the ordinary larrikin
rarely or never does—Le., interfere with public interest
and enjoyment, and hamper the efforts of performers.
However, one good result will follow the bearish efforts of
these humorless and utterly unoriginal buffoons; the
public will grasp the fact that a lot of vulgar and witless,
though privileged, savages are being coddled up chiefly at
the State expense. It is stated that some of these
creatures were attired as women. Next time they appear
at a theatre unmuzzled it is to be hoped that Mr. Musgrove
will order the gaol-van for eight o’clock punctually.

part merely by reading it over. In one night, for a wager,
he learnt right off Scribe’s play of “Le Terre d’Eau,”
one of the longest and most intricate in any 'language.

The fault in the acting of J. L. Toole’s company is the
same as was found under the direction of old Boucicault—
a lack of spontaniety and impulse. They are drilled to
death, and become a lor of wooden marionettes, while
Toole’s own acting, like Boticicault.’s, has become very
precise and calculated. Jr is like Alick Batinermans
hatting—One says: “We want this game to be a lively
game, not a slow, stolid business.”

That rara am*, an actor who is not a masher, has, ii is
alleged, been discovered in the person.of Edwin Booth, on
whom at his hotel two young and foolish maidens called
and sent up their cards. He did not refuse to see them,
but, descending to the parlour, in freezing tones enquired
their business, and being told that they had seen him play
the night before and would like to make his acquaintance,
at once proceeded to point out their indiscretion and the
danger attending feminine pursuit, of actors. Then, look
ing at the cards, this stern moralist said: “These name-
are of course assumed—if I knew your proper names, f
should certainly inform your parents !” and then bowed
the abashed damsels out. This is an excellent “ ad.”

iine of old men, with his Sir Peter Teazle, Col. Damns, and

In England sympathisers with the relics
of the Balaclava charge have taken the trouble, to
hunt them up, and found 22 of the common soldiers,
every one of them in the depth of poverty. The
workhouse is the superannuated soldier’s home.

Siberian prisons is Colonel Hurst, of Washington.

effect of George Kennan’s famous articles is simply
delicious, And it is not to be forgotten that
when Kennan and Frost began their tour they did
so as disbelievers in the atrocity of the adminis-
trative exile system.

‘Old Tom.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Conspicuous amongst the “ originals ” so fre-
quently met with at the old-time rushes was a
tall, spare, elderly man with a slight stoop and a
long, full, white board. Of a kindly disposition
and quiet demeanour, “Old Tom,” as he was
usually designated, was liked and respected by all
who knew him. He was an enthusiastic spiri-
tualist, and although silent and reserved in general
conversation, would hold forth by the hour on his
favourite doctrine, whenever he could get a syin
pathetic audience. He was generally considered
to be “ oft’ his chump,” as the diggers phrased it,
and, in view of his antecedents, they were pro-
bably not far wrong. Be that as it may, many a
merry hour they enjoyed at what came in time to
he known as old Tom's “services.” He had a
small annuity, sufficient for his few requirements
and frugal habits, and so lie had ample leisure to
follow the bent of his inclinations. These lay
chiefly in using the “ planchette," whereby ho had
accumulated a vast pile of manuscript, which,
being all but illegible, might fairly be described as
being “of no use to any one but the owner.”
As might be expected, he had to put up with a
lot of chaff now and then, but beyond an occa-
sional “ Weel, Tom, old man, hoo aboot the
sperrits noo?” and the like, which good-humoured
interrogatories Tom would answer by a kindly
nod and a twinkle in his grey eye, his general in-
offensiveness and simplicity of character compelled
consideration from the most thoughtless or
facetiously inclined. On line days he would come
sauntering up the “lead," and seating himself on
a log open out a package of manuscript, much to
the delight of the surrounding “ shepherds,” who,
sc- nting a “ service,” would speedily gather about
the old man from all the adjacent claims.
“ < fentlemen,” he would, begin, “with your per-
mission I will read a
few of my latest p’anche
grams.”
Tom’s funny pronuncia-
tion of this new com-
pound of his own coinage
would invariably evoke
a burst of laughter, and
amid a chorus of en-
couraging cries to “Eire
away, o 1 d brus h e r, ”
“Wire in, Tom,’’ and
so forth —he would go
through his paper in
quiet earnest tones ; and
being a good elocutionist
would usually command
th e at ten tion of his h carers
till ho had finished read-
ing, when he would wind
up with a dissertation
on the “ spheres ” or
kindred matter, until
shepherding hours were
o' - — "ud it was time to
go nome. But the “ ser-
vices ” were not always so
decorqusly condncted.
< Iccasionally the paper he
chanced to read would
contain so many absurdly
ludicrous images that the
uproarious demonstra-
tions of his congregation
would prove too much
for even old Tom’s patient
equanimity, and he would
reluctantly roll up his
papers and depart with
a grave and sorrowful
mien.
The venerable figure of
old Tom is no longer to
lie met with on the
‘ leads,” for he has gone
to those “spheres” he
so dearly loved to talk
of. I will now relate a
brief passage in his life,
which may serve'to throw
some light on the vagaries
of his later years.
Part Second.
Many years ago, in the good old times when
“Old Tom’’was still young and gold plentiful
and much easier to find than now-a-days—worse
luck —he and his brother Dick had a rich claim on
one of the great alluvial rushes of the day. The
depth of the shaft was (ill feet, and the brothers
had hired a casual acquaintance named Ben. to
assist at the windlass ; having now nearly worked
out, they had plenty of stowage-room, so, ex-
cept for the wash-dirt, the hauling was over, and
they all worked below.
Having a roomy tent, the three men lived
together, and, to save time, it was customary for
Dun to go home and prepare dinner, and when it
was ready to return and call the others. On a cer-
tain day he went home as usual, leaving Dick at
work knocking down washdirt, A very little way
behind him Ben. was stowing mullock, and ram-
ming it home by means of a short heavy wooden
rammer—with the twofold object of securing the
roof and economising room. The “ dirt” proved
exceptionally rich, and Dick told Ben. to come
and see a nugget he had just picked out. Ben.
crawled over, taking the rammer with him, and
looked at the gold. It was about the size of
a hen’s egg slightly flattened. Whilst Dick, with
head bent-down, was admiringly regarding it, and
conjecturing its weight, Ben., drawing back a
I (tic and raising the hammer, dealt him a tremen-
dous blow on the nape of the neck. Without a
sound the body sank on its side. Ben. took off
his cap, wiped the perspiration from his brow,
and, stretching out his arm, turned the dead man
on his back, and looked at him attentively. Raising
his shirt he undid a broad chamois-leather belt
with pockets surrounding it at intervals —an
article in common use by the diggers of the days
when banks were not—and pulling it fmm under
the body fastened it round Iris own. He listened.
The hook was jingling against the end of the
shaft, Tom was shaking the rope- the signal for
dinner. Shouting'up the usual “all right!” he
went to the “stowage” and threw out rapidly
about a cartload of stuff. In the cavity lie placed
the dead Hick in a sitting posture, and, having
made scarcely room enough, leaned his back
against a prop, and with his feet jammed the
limbs hard up. Then he threw back the stuff,
covering up his handiwork thoroughly, squared
up the margin of the mullock, and ran his shovel
lightly over the soft rock floor, carefully obliterat-
ing any unusual marks. After a final searching
look all round, to make quite certain he had over-
looked nothing, lie put the nugget in his pocket,
extinguished the light, and went up the shaft.
When he got home he told Tom that his brother
had taken a pick to the. smith to get laid and
steeled, and as ho might begone for a considerable
time they need not wait for him. Accordingly,
dinner over, the two men returned to work. On
their way back they passed a crowd looking at a
dog-fight; a man stepped out and accosted Ben.,
but Tom kept on to the claim and went down the
shaft.
Now, during the interval of the dinner-hour a
curious thing quite unforeseen by Ben. had hap-
pened. Owing to the relaxation of the muscles
one of the dead man’s limbs had straightened out.
Therefore when Tom lit his candle and began to
crawl along the drive his eye all at once fell upon
this strange object protruding from the mullock.
He stopped—fascinated, failing to comprehend.
For a second or two he remained motionless, as a
man who comes suddenly upon a snake- then, as
the fearful truth of murder broke upon him, he
drew back into the shaft, confused and terrified,
for lie felt that his own life, too, hung trembling
in the balance. At that moment, the oscillating
rope and some falling particles of rock, proclaimed
that Ben. was coming down. By a supreme effort
Tom subdued his agitation, and springing into
HOW IS I T HONE?
ON THE BEACH.
IN THE BATH.
the steps, shouted to Ben. in as steady tones as he
could command, to go back, that he had left his
purse on the table, and was coming up
to go home for it. Ben., confident that ail
was secure below, returned to the top, while
Tom, with great difficulty, continued his ascent.
On reaching the surface he rested for a moment,
averting his head to hide his grim resolve and the
tell-tale pallor of his face. Then, with a quick
glance around to make sure of assistance, he
scrambled to his feet, and making a desperate
rush at Ben., bore him to the ground, shouting - ,
“Help! murder! help!” The diggers in the
vicinity ran to the spot and seized the murderer.
Tom's fast-failing strength enabled him to tell his
story ere darkness fell upon him, and they carried
him away for medical aid.
M hen I say that the severe mental shock he
had sustained necessitated his removal to an
asylum for a few months; that Ben. was executed,
and that the money and gold for which he bar-
tered his life, proved the source of Tom’s income
during the remainder of his days, I think I have
told all 1 know about “ Old Tom.”
J. LYALL.

Forgotten History.
An Atrocity Story.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A station I was once on in the Far North of
Australia bore the reputation of being the scrub-
biest in the continent. I won't name it, too many
have lost money over it; but when I mention the
fact that it was nearly all gidyea and brigalow
scrub, and that there was no part of the run
where you could see more than a hundred yards
before you, many an old Northerner will recognise
the place.
The station next to us was bossed by a man
with a “ black ” theory. He came from a colony
where the blacks had long since disappeared
and was full of the idea that he alone knew the
proper way of managing the natives, and that
everybody else was a fool; this sort of delusion
being common to now comers. At that time the
blacks were in the dangerous transition <stato
between full-blown savagery and semi-civilisation.
On some stations they were allowed in, at others
they wove; kept out; and so they just knew enough
of the white man's habits to make them more
dangerous than ever. The manager aforesaid had,
of course, let them come in and camp about the
place in order to exercise his civilising influence
on them, but on our place they were rigorously
kept out.
We were camped about eight miles from the
homestead at a large tailing-yard, -making a
general muster, when one day a young jackeroo
from the station where the blacks were allowed
in, accompanied by another new-chum, rode up in
hot haste. As it happened it was Sunday morn-
ing, and, with the exception of the men tailing
the cattle, we were all in camp. His story was
soon told. The theoretical manager and the mar-
ried couple cooking on the next place had been
murdered by the niggers. The two owed their
escape to having been away on the run. When
they came back they found that all the blacks had
decamped, leaving three dead white bodies as an
evidence of their unholy visit.
In a short time we were on the way back with
them ; the manager, myself, a stockman, and two
black boys—one of them a deserter from the
native police. It was a horrible sight. All the
bodies had been most shamefully and barbarously
mutilated, the unfortunate super, worst of all.
Every indignity that the savages could think of
had been wreaked on his corpse.
The plan of campaign was soon arranged. Our
manager had too much work on hand with the
muster to leave, so he stopped with the two men
to bury the bodies, and P , the stockman, and
I and the two boys started after the blacks.
The old trooper was a splendid tracker, and for
the first ten miles we could follow as fast as our
horses could get along. The trail took us back
near our own homestead, and, fearing they might
have doubled back to pay that a visit, we' rode in
but found everything quiet, so stopped there that
night. Next morning, by piccaninny daylight, we
were off again, and soon came to the first camp of
the night before. It was, of course, deserted
though several beef-bones left behind showed they
had been doing a bit of cattle-killing on the road.
I I lie track from this camp led straight to a tract
of wild country at the back that joined what was
then called “the desert,” on the road to B
Downs. This no-man’s land consisted of a low
tableland, mostly spinifex ; the edges being sand-
stone cliffs of no great height, but very broken.
It was scrub the whole way, and about three
o clock I red., the old trooper, heard a tomahawk
some distance ahead. After that we had to go
slow 7 as it was probable that some of the natives
would still be making for the camp, and we might
run across them. At last, after exercising great
patience, we came in sight of the darkies, on some
open ground not far from the foot of the sand-
stone cliff ; I had just taken a hasty glance at the
surroundings, when a wild yell from an old gin
close at hand told that they were aware of us.
They had evidently camped near the cliff on pur-
pose, for they made for the rocks at once and dis-
appeared amongst the fallen boulders. I ran one
(11 fellow down, and then, seeing P ’s horse
r derless at the edge of the rocks and Fred, just
disappearing behind a boulder, I jumped off my
horse and commenced to climb towards the cliff as
well. A most strange silence suddenly fell; f or
minute there had been the outcries of the blmks
our own shots, and the noise of our horses. Now’
although I knew that there must be some thirty
or forty natives around, I could not hear a sound
I was peering about on all sides, when suddenly
a couple of pistol-shots were fired close to me, anil
a grizzled-looking old black crept out on
hand and knees and sank down with a groan
Fred., who appeared immediately afterwards*
grinned as he pointed to the blood pumping np
gouts between the black’s shoulder-blades. To-
gether we scrambled np a big boulder in front of
us and found ourselves almost at the foot of j|„.
cliff’s and witnesses of a strange sight-.
The cliffs were not high, but were precipitous
and only to be scaled easily in a few places.
ot these spots was in front of us. It, was amu row
cleft only a foot or two wide, and the blacks were
.mounting by shinning np witli a foot on each -id,..
They were scrambling up as iiard as they could,
and their speed was increased by.some hasty shots
from Fred, and myself. The foremost one had
his hands on the top and was just drawing himself
up when Fred, gave a yell of delight. There was
P ,of whom I had lost sight, running along the
top of the cliff with his revolver in one hand and a
uulla-nulla in the other. The leading blackfcllow
had just straightened himself up and another was
at his heels, when P suddenly greeted him
with a terrible whack on the head that .smashed
the. nulla and sent the native back into the cleft
where, he jammed and blocked all the others.
p fold us that just as we emerged into the
open he noticed two gins slip away by them, elves
into the rocks. After the firH scrimmage lie got
off his horse like the rest and followed t hem,
picking np a nulla-nulla as he went, for his re-
volver was empty. _ He was just in time to catch
sight of the gins disappearing on the top of the
cliff. _ Following, he found an easy ascent, and in
the nick of time was able to cut off the retreat of
most of the men, the principal murderers.
When we came back to the camp and got our
horses the boys amused themselves by routing out
the gins who had taken refuge in the holes and
avities, and mustered up between twenty and
thirty of them.
“ You see this,” said Fred, approaching with a
dilly-bag in his hand. He emptied the contents
on a piece of bark. Amongst the rubbish was a
piece of dark-coloured flesh, quite unrecognisable
to us.
Fred grinned and pointed to his side; “White
fellow,” he said.
It was a piece of the murdered superintendent’s
kidney.
Some time after that we were near the scene,
and turned in to have a look at it. The natives
had never revisited the spot, and still wedged
head-down wards in the cliff was the dried corpse
of the blackfellow P had knocked down. Poor
P ! Shortly after that he went out with a
mob of cattle to stock country in the Gulf, and
died of fever in that delectable region.
F>.
[Our correspondent is a calm and dispassionate person,
not, however, more cold-blooded than lots of people in the
far North, where a blackfellow’s life is no more sacred
than that of a sheep. We should like to hear the other
side of this story.— Ed. B.]
“La Grippe.”
THE SYMPTOMS OF THE DISEASE.
Unfortunately the symptoms of the disease are
only too well known. First there are chills,
speedily accompanied by headache and tightness
across the forehead ; tenderness and watering
from the eyes, and discharge from the nostrils.
The bodily temperature is raised, the pulse becomes
cpiick and moist. The digestive organs become
impaired, appetite is partially lost, and thirst,
vomiting and occasional diarrhoea are induced.
Speedily the whole physique is weakened and the
spirits depressed, this lasting for two or three
days, when convalescence begins, unless the attack
be of unusual severity, or the person be old and
feeble. On the appearance of the disease in
England, the Pall Mall Gazette interviewed
the three leading London medical men in such
diseases, viz. : Stk Oscar Clayton, Sib Morell
Mackenzie and Dr. Robson Roose, and each
prescribed stimulating or tonic treatment. La
Grippe is a constitutional matter, it is not merely
local, and the local effects and symptoms are only
the outward signals that the constitution is
deranged. On the approach of the disease.
Clements Tonic should be taken. This remedy
goes at once to the root of the trouble and cubes
the cause, and t his is the only way to treat La
Grippe. Taking cough mixtures to cure the
running from the nose, &c., is perfectly useless,
the treatment must—positively must—be stimu-
lating and restorative, and Clements Tonic is an
ideal preparation for this purpose, and it has
cured thousands of cases within the last 6 weeks
and saved its patrons thousands of pounds in
doctor’s bills.
Read this letter : “ Sir,—Four years ago I caught
a severe cold which settled in my head and chest,
and caused an incessant cough and copious dis-
charge. of phlegm ; this brought on great weakness
and debility. After considerable attention and
doctors' treatment, the cough was relieved and
finally cured, but the debility remained and
seemed to defy the skill of the medical men and
the power of medicine. 1 had no appetite and
could scarcely eat a particle of food, and was
almost reduc-d to a skeleton*, having entirely
wasted away. I had taken pints Of- cod-liver oil,
malt extracts, and other medicines without
relief, when I was recommended to take Clements
Tonic. I procured a bottle, and am thankful to
say that 1 immediately began to improve ; I could
eat better, and relish and digest what I did eat.
1 gained flesh every day, and my strength rapidly
returned, and a'ter six week’s treatment with
Clements Tonic, I felt anew woman altogether.
I took Clements Tonic six months ago, and have
never had any sickness since, and am only too
glad to add my name to your list of grateful
sufferers. Tours sincerely, Mary Kellett.
Johnny Burgess is now the hero of Wil-
cannia. The other day he crossed the Tallywalka,
which was fully four miles wide, with the mail-
trap and four horses. At times all that was
visible was the heads of the swimming horses and
the top half of Burgess, who was standing on the
seat of the completely-submerged trap. This per-
formance probably puts up a colonial record, not
excepting the performances in river-crossing of
Hungry Tyson, who was a noted man at the game
in his day.

A LONG-PELT WANT.
What is the meaning of this lot? the thoughtful and intelligent reader naturally asks:
Well, it is nothing more nor less than a device to ensure the safe arrival of an affianced
best girl ordered “from home” by the average Australian young man (carriage paid). Said
affianced bride, it is well known, too frequently makes other arrangements on voyage-in
point of fact, marries one of the officers or something.
The above picture shows the patent Jumna-Levi mash-proof packing-ease in which a
bride may be shipped with tolerable certainty of coming to hand.
Henry George’s weakness —apart from liis
Single-tax theory—is cigar-smoking. We do not
think we have yet seen the great-little man off the
platform without a weed in his mouth. Georges
hat acts as an extinguisher—it makes him look
ordinary, even commonplace. But when lie bares
his head he is a noble-looking man.

who died, a few years ago from the effects of a
railway - collision, was a great amateur actor.
Once he was playing with the late Mrs. Stoneham
in a scene where the two had to embrace. While
they were clasped in one another s arms Mrs.
Stoneham murmured gently to Alfred : Oh, th s
is Bliss !”
Carlyle used to keep three sovereigns in a
little packet on his mantlepieoe, which he called
“ Leigh Hunt sovereigns,’’because the latter occa-
sionally borrowed them. Carlyle was wont to
narrate the circumstance to all whom it.did not
concern. Hunt would have lent Carlyle three
thousand sovereigns had he possessed them, ana
never disclosed the circumstance.

on to the vested interests of those spiritual pluto-
crats who maintain that the World cannot be
saved without giving them from £ooo to bIOOO a
year, with free mansions and trips to V urrup.

Melbourne Law Courts to study lawyers heads
and faces and report thereon to the Lv kmnu
Herald. Speaking of Samuel St. John iopp, \
said he had “come across the rubicund Mr. 1 opp.
When Samuel read this he thoughtfully remarked
to Hood, “ Well, I’m stout no doubt, but J don t
think I'm rubicund.” Oh, said Hood, it must
be a misprint for Rubicon. He meant to say that
you had crossed the Rubicon of the profession.
Someone sends us the foregoing as a choice mot.
Had we heard it before, we should have at once
prophesied that Hood had been marked out for the

draws cackles from that servile crew, the junior
Bar.

"v The disgust and contempt produced by the
methods of the British naval court-martial in
Sydney have done more to alienate sympathy
from Imperial institutions than all the democratic
leader-writing and platform-talking done during
the last ten years. It is per medium of incidents
of this sort that Australians ai’e made to feel that
the ways of the ruling-classes of England are not,
and never can be, our ways.

to his leader, to whom he spoke but on two oc-
casions during the whole time he remained m the
House.

H. E, Downer, the new S.A. Attorney-
General, was appointed for the simple reason tha t
the Cockburn Ministry couldn’t get anybody else-
He is a smart lawyer, but is unpopular as a poli-
tician. There are three brothers of this family
practising the law in Adelaide —John, Henry, and
George. Harold Downer, one of the most brilliant
lawyers South Australia has known, died some
years ago, just when he had worked up a large prac-
tice.

l tie Sydney newspapers all pronounce the
parliamentary speech in which Parkes introduced
his Federation Convention proposals last week to
have been a splendid success. But ask any news-
paper-man or any politician who heard the oration
his opinion on the subject. In truth, the old man
has never in all his life made a more feeble, com-
monplace disjointed, or illogical utterance. Ihe
speech was utterly unworthy of a great occasion.
However, the good old daily Sup-Press carefully
cover up their present idol’s intellectual naked-
ness with their broad-sheets.
General Miribel is appointed Co.mmander-
iu-Chief of the French array, but be has to abstain
from Boulangising. Here is a man unknown
almost to the entire world, yet the events of a
day or two may bring his name in everyone s
mouth. But there is always the temptation to act
the Napoleon. Gambetta would go for his own
hand, so would Boulanger, so would Carnot, if he
were strong enough. Moral —Never trust a poli-
tician, but insist on your representative being your
delegate. Take a turn now and again at being
your own representative, and let the other man do
the voting. Otherwise lie’ll round on you.

Parkes claims the credit of having opposed
the Soudan movement. He probably opposed it
simply because he couldn t claini the kudos ol
having originated the idea. Speaking at Glasgow
not long previously, he ventured to say that it
ever the old country needed military assistance
she would get it from Australia. - He thus first
incited and then denounced a picturesque act
which he would fain have placed at his own credit
iu the pages of history—pages which, as Parkes
well knows, often give to a merely dramatic and
meretricious act, a prominence denied to the
sterling and self-denying deeds of true patriotism.

What is the moral of the detention in
Darlinghurst Gaol of the unfortunate man who
has just been released after suffering nearly four
years’ imprisonment for the non-payment of the
sum of £lB (is. ? The moral is simply this-that
the soi-dimnt democratic community o' N.S.W.
stands listlessly by while the lawyer’s trades-
union (of which the judges are the chief officers)
put a stone under the screw and endeavour to
squeeze blood out of it. No debtor is ever cruelly
imprisoned for four years unless for the crime cf
neglecting or being unable to pay a lawyer’s bill
of costs. This was the offence for which this poor
wretch has wasted in prison some of the best years
of his life.
# # *
The Australian Star, in objecting to the
appointment of Oscar Meyer to superintend the
N.S.W. court at the London Mining Exhibition,
speaks of him as a “foreigner.” If it comes to
that, half our population are foreigners—some of
the Star directors are foreigners. A Sydney
Sunday paper follows suit in denouncing, on
“ Australian grounds,” Meyer’s selection. Is there
any special virtue in having been born on the soil of
Australia ? Oscar Meyer may not have been born
at Croajinga’ong or Jamberoo, but, better still, he
is a good cosmopolitan who, having seen all coun-
tries and having learnt to speak most of the
world’s languages, has elected to be a citizen of
Australia.
This case of the
Egeria irritates a some-
what sore point in Aus-
tralian cities where we
are accustomed to see
the victims of arbi-
trary naval punishment
marched through the
streets to the public
gaols. There is always
a feeling of resentment
against this, for here are
our fellow-citizens herded
with felons, but under a
totally different law to
our own. Such a state
of things cannot last.

By the way, judging
from the fact that the
alleged Egeria mutineers
were packed off to
England last week, no inquiry is to be I;eld at

Egeria’s officers. The British naval authorities
acted with much discretion in not having the seven
convicted sailors confined inSydney prison. Had
the men been sent to Darlinghurst, the result
would have been a memorable chapter in Austra-
lian history.
* • *
The death of Mr. Andrew Chirnside, the
Werribee millionaire, recalls the fact that within
the last few years the Chirnsides have posed as
benevolent gentlemen, and one of the late Mr.
Thomas Chirnside's particular benevolences was
purchasing a site —now worth £2OOO in the town-
ship of Wyndham for a Presbyterian church. Ihe
congregation backed up his large donation
liberally, and a promise of a settlement of the
deeds to trustees followed. No such settlement
was made, and church and ground passed with
the rest of the property to Mr. Andrew. He it
is charitably supposed in the hurry of business
forgot to fulfil his brother's intentions, and now
he is dead, and a state of uncertainty pervades
that congregation. The ground, you see, is not
theirs, the church is doubtful—they are assured
that “the church will be a church as long as
there is .a Chirnside on the Werribee’ w.uch
utterance is just about as vague as t he declaration
of the Maori prophet that “ the potato is
cooked.” What are the heirs’ intentions in the
matter? Suppose that, te epted by an offer of a
few hundreds on the bargain, they should sell out,
sav, to the Roman Catholic party ! Anyway, the
church stands as a memorial to the honour and
glory of—the Ohirnsides.
# *'
Arrangements have been made to “ sweat
Francis Ormond’s estate in the Melbourne law
courts. The charities, which benefit so largely
under the simple interpretation of the dead man’s
will, have taken out a summons for the purpose of
asserting their claim to the £140,000 residue in-
tended to be apportioned among private
Nobody supposes for a moment that the charities
trustees have any chance to get more than their
respective whacks of Ormond’s property. It is
barely conceivable that they can dream of clutching
that £140,000. But as the summons will “in-
volve exhaustive legal arguments,” the cost of
which comes out of the Ormond estate, anyway,
the whole business may be regarded as a compli-
mentary feed to the dressed-up sharks whose
protected jaws work in the interests of Humbug.

A caveat has been lodged against the will
of David Berry, of Coolangatta, who whiled away
the last moments of his 102nd year in bequeathing
a million sterling to John Hay-
Old David died the other day,
And o’er the Styx was ferried ;
But now there is the dooce to pay
He made a will when, growlers say,
His mind had crumbled in decay,
And didn’t know a “ yea ’’ from “ nay. ”
Yet where is proof convincing, pray V
It pleased him, as he passed away,
To think “in death I’m making Hay.”
Contented quite, he turned to clay
And soon was David berried.
# * *
The maddest woman in Melbourne is Mrs.
Bluett, who, after swearing that a certain corpse
was that of her mysteriously-disappeared ne’er-do-
well of a husband, and then affectionately paying
the corpse’s funeral expenses, has had her old man
handed over to her as a deserter by the local
police. “ Who is to pay the undertaker’s account ?”
she asks piteously.

The manner in which Lady Hopetoun’s
Irish nationality is ignored in Melbourne is very
remarkable. At the Eight-Hours’ sports half-a-
dpzen bare-legged Highlanders danced and yelled
like maniacs in the presence of the Scottish Hope-
toun and his Countess, but there was no Irish jig
LOYALTY IN THE BACK COUNTRY,
“GEE UP, HOPETOUN!!” (whack.)
or “country dance” in honour of “me Lady.
Then, again, when the Earl and his sprightly (ion-
sort visited Prahran in state, in Punt-road they
passed under a line of Hags stretched across the
street and in which every conceivable flag was
included except the green one ! It is said that the
secret of all this is that the Countess has given the
snub direct to some of the colonial toadies and
their missuses who thought they could crawl about
Government House as in the good old days of
“ dear Lady Loch.”

Dear Bulletin,— The Beg,a people want
to know who poisoned their dogs. I did. Why i
To save my own life. I wanted sleep. Why
didn’t I clear out of the town ? Because it didirt
suit me. Human beings, I take it, have still
superior rights to dogs. No man has a right to
keep a dog if it inflicts injury on any human
being.—E.J.J.

# * *
We read that Mrs. Sophia Laramore. <>f
Waterbary, U.S., has just finished a curious tnor-
tusry wreath in memory of her late husband. It
is made of relies of that, gentleman and of Ibe
wives who preceded Imr Cor it seem, that Mr.
Laramore was strongly addicted to matrimony).
The frame is of putty, into which, while soft, the
widow has put a heterogeneous collection of
articles, such as Mr. Laramore’s spectacles,
his pill-bottle, and many articles which he
had treasured during life, such as a jack-
knife, a piece of candy, &c., buttons from
his old clothing, and a small bottle containing
cheese made by his first wife. Enclosed in the
frame is a picture, which is surmounted by a
device in flowers and leaves, made entirely of
pieces of cloth, &c., drawn from the late Mr.
Laramore’s clothing, and the shirt in which he
died is used to compose a fearful and wonderful
bird. Hair watch-chains and teeth go to make up
the rest of this most strange and tasteful thing in
wreaths.
# * *
Apart from the nonsensical gush always
poured out over a clergyman’s death, there is much
to regret in the loss of Rev. .1. F. Ewing, of
Toorak, though he has been simply killed by over-
study and lack of exercise. His sermons are
praised up as inspirations, but they were awful
dead-weights upon Toorakia. In sooth he was an
able man, who lost himself in the maze of words,
words, words, and it is a pity to see such valuable
lives thrown away on the straw-chopping logic of
theology, merely under the temptation of large
salaries.

# * *
Everyone in Melbourne sympathises with
Mr. Call, the veteran Police-Magistrate, for the
awful affliction Mrs. Gall being burnt to death
which has hastened his retirement from official
life. Mr. Call is, perhaps, the best beak in Aus-
tralia, but nothing can ever make the draconian
Panton an ideal magistrate. He has a most mis-
chievous fancied gift of being able to read faces,
like Lavater, which saves him the trouble of listen-
ing to cases. Robt. Brough, in the “ Magistrate,”
took Call off well in the long magisterial side-
boards and his habit of pulling them.

A young and enthusiastic minister in a
Western district (Vie.) church, in the course of a
recent sermon, broke out thus: “ A prayerful
Christian is what I call a green Christian. I

* * *
Did Lady Carington really compose that
now memorable letter to Mrs. Brough ? Of course
she didn’t. We all know and admire Lady
Carington as a pretty, gracious little lady with a
sempiternal smile of good nature. It is sheer
cruelty, not to say the greatest of nonsense, to in-
duce any unsophisticated simple-minded little
creature to append her name to a ponderous pro-
duction which everybody knows she could never
possibly have written. You might as well pre-
tend to recognise as authentic the signature of
Aaron Fregalsky, the loan and discount banker,
to a cheque for £25,000 in aid of a fund to enable
people to dispute the legality of usury, as credit
Lady Carington with the authorship of so grandi-
loquent and unwomanish a literary production.

Little Lord Hopctouu can ho an unconscious
humourist when he doesn’t try. Last week he,
wandered down amongst the wilds of Collingwood,
to tell the mayor, corporation, and destitute
female children that “ he could not wish the local
Home of I lope better luck than to quote the motto
of his own family— At *pas non frorta.” The effect
of this Latin quotation upon Collingwood was
startling. The destitute little girls sat wondering
whether it meant that the “Cuviner was agoin’ to
give'em something,” while the mayor and other
aristocracy delivered “cheers for his I (excellency
and no fracture" until the viceregal party had
disappeared from view. After which there was a
special meeting of the Council, and a resolu ion
(jailing upon the Town Clerk to buy a 1 rench
dictionary was carried with great vigour. An
active search for that there bit of learnm’ is now
being made through the dictionary, and the battled
explorers are buoyed by the* belief that they are
sure to tind it presently. Meantime, the savants
of Collingwood are inclined to think that Hope-
toun’s family motto means. “Don’t fracture my
best ’at.”

A Melbourne broker is trying to boat a
new liquor, alleged to be better than whisky or
brandy. The question is, how much of it does a
man have to drink before he can commence to see
things crawling over the bed ?
# * *
A Melbourne firm of printers are endea-
vouring to make the way of the transgressor hard
by the manufacture of “preventative ” cheques.
The most promising of these novelties seems to be
a tinted cheque, the colour of which is warranted
to fly under the application of any acid “ink-
eradicator.” This device will double up the man
who has been calculating on changing “two
pounds ten ” into “five hundred”; but we cant
see that it will prevent the development of eight
and 8 into eighty and 80.

That blue-blooded weed, Prince Albert
Victor, is back again in England, with his passion
for the pretty Teck cousin burning as feebly as
ever. The Queen won’t hear of an engagement,
Wales and wife disapprove, the maiden says that
a nunnery would be far nicer, and, take it
altogether, Albert Victor's love affair is making
everybody mad, himself included. Tummy’s
equerries have been instructed to hold the flabby
vioe-Heir-Apparent under the pump, but it’s no
good. When he has dried himself and changed
his collar, etc., he maunders around moaning “ O!
I would give half grandma’s kingdom just for one
little kith.”

paper to his brother leeches and sawbones touch-
ing the shameful manner in which the hospitals

The married heroine of the “ Melbourne
Morgue Mystery” was undoubtedly a single
woman, according to medical testimony at the in-
quest. The doctor who insisted upon the cer-
tainty of this doubtful fact is now taking shelter
behind the further fact that the lady’s husband
went mad after marriage. It is also supposed
that she went mad herself. Either the husband,
wife, or doctor must have beer l mad. This is a
mad world.
* & #
“Nothing but the living presence of the
lady of whom we write happily shatters the per-
fect chain of evidence, and proves that the care-
fully-nurtured daughter of a gallant soldier, and
the ward of one who is deservedly respected in the
community, has not in the waters of the bay
sought surcease for sorrow upon the death of her
deeply lamented mother.” There, now! In that
greasy flow of language did the Melbourne Stan-
dard explain that a live girl hadn’t committed
suicide while not suffering from temporary in-
sanity. Thereafter followed the chain of evidence
which the wild romancer had happily shattered
beforehand. It reminds one of the poetic story of
“Maud Muller,” written backwards, and begin-
ning with “it might have been.”

The Morgue (Melb.) Mystery was finally
identified by her false teeth —
That Mystery to solve they sought
For many days in vain ;
Detectives thought they really ought
To dig it up again ;
But now ’twill be allowed to lie
The peaceful award beneath —
They know poor Mrs. Roberts by
Her composition teeth.
Of tragedies in human life
The Morgue can stories tell
The doctor swore this much-wrung wife
Was but a single belle ;
And yet, it seems, a year ago
She wore the bridal wreath,
Whose mouth is closed for ever, though
They recognise her teeth.

The puzzle of the Collins-street Block just
now is a young lady with thick, short, blonde
hair, all in close and crispy, crimpy curded tiny
curls, like a nigger’s, the question being whether
this is a natural or artificial product. The mys-
tery is as great as that of Beau Brummel’s
collars, only solved by “Starch is the Man.”
Since the Boy Girl Was the sensation there has
been nothing so problematical as this What
Is It?

It is remarked by the acquaintances of the
Sydnev man who has become so suddenly and so
enormously rich that he sees hardly anybody now.

remote as though he saw “ a cock-fight in a distant
place. ”

A couple of hundred helpless patients are
housed in the so-called Sydney Hospital—a miser-
able wooden shed which, when it catches fire, wdl
burn right down in a quarter of an hour. Succes-
sive Governments have dallied for years over the
matter of providing decent accommodation for the
sick poor of the central district. In our mind’s
ear, Horatio, do we hear the patients bubble and
hiss in their beds while Premier Parkes stands on
the other side of the road squeezing out of his
beautiful beard with both hands the human fat
borne on the balmy breeze. The “ hospital ’
building is such a tinder-box that it may any day
be fired by a bad case of inflammation.

The Chaftey Brothers are trying the ex-
periment of flooding out the rabbit-burrows. In
one or two which have been since opened properly
arranged embankments have been found behind
which the rabbits were waiting for the flood to go
down. Now what will they say if such stories
get into the English papers ?

Two Melbourne identities were colloguing
the other day, and said one : “ Wonder if Byrne,
of the Exchange, has gone to his own funeral
yet?” The point of this lies in the fact that
Byrne, best known of boni faces, used to go to a
funeral almost every day of the week, and you
would scarce see one without his melancholy
visage glaring through a mourning-coach window.
His only explanation was “Me good nature.”
The more one thinks of the scheme of the
Victorian Bible-in-Schools League, the more
absurd it looks. The regular school-teachers, in-
fidels, and all, are to dose the scholars daily with
Scripture reading, and naturally the child will ask
the teacher to have some points cleared up, but
the mouths both of the believing teacher and the
unbelieving teacher are to be hermetically sealed.
The mere statement of the thing shows how
ridiculous and unfeasible it is. But then it is the
thin end of the wedge of a big conspiracy.
In a late issue of the Barrier Miner the
editor states that a prominent Sydney Single-
taxer has offered to supply literary matter at five
shillings per column. The journalist assailed
asserts that his offer was for the contribution of a
stereotyped circular letter of which copies would
be supplied to various country papers, and that
viewed from this standpoint his terms are above
rather than below the usual rates. The man whose
necessities compel him to supply articles to the
country papers of N.S. Wales is in a mighty bad
fix. The rate of pay is low, but then that is of
little consequence, as the “copy ” is rarely paid
for.
1 he Most N oble the Marquis of Ailesbury
had the cheek to give before the French correc-
tional tribunal evidence as to character m favour
of his old pal, “Jubilee Juggins” Benzon, and

Writing of men and things in Morocco, a
Mr. Walter Harris is unintentionally rough on the
blessings of missionary enterprise and Christian
civilisation in the wrong places. He says : ' the
Moors live in peace. Crime with them is not
nearly so rife as crime with uNow let us, tor a
moment only, Christianise the whole Moorish
nation. What is the result? The natives let
their hair grow, instead of shaving their heads ;
the result is disease. They drink wine; the result
is crime with which they are unable to cope on
account of possessing no police —and drunkenness
and death. They eat pork, and suffer from scrofula
and horrible diseases at once.” Mr. Harris might
have added—they introduce “morality,” and are
immediately confronted with “ the social evil,” or,
they get married by a parson, and want a Divorce
Court. But enough is mentioned to show that
Morocco, as Morocco, is best left alone. Why
take the blessed Terewth into a country which lias
hitherto managed to do without police ? Let the
Moors live and die without being guilty of “ in-
sulting behaviour.”
Dear Bulletin, Now that F. W. Ward
has vacated the editorship of the Sydney Daily
Telegraph, it is no more than just that there
should be s; id of him this : That no man who
has been seat ;d in the editorial chair of an Aus-
tralian daily paper in this generation had a truer
sense of the dignity of his profession, or did more
to encourage the literary side, as distinguished
from news-reporting, of journalism. Ardent
Freetrader though he was, he did not carry his
fiscal principles into his management of the paper.
Many a time and oft he could have obtained an
article “in the cheapest market” by pilfering
it — aH do many other Freetrade editors—but
wherever practicable, the proprietary’s guineas
went into the pockets of men. In this he
was doing no more than acting wisely in the
paper’s interests, for its virility and success were
largely due to his policy in this direction ; but,
nevertheless, he might have done differently and
still done all that was seemingly according to the
virtues of newspaper management, traditional in
the offices of too many of the daily newspapers.
There is not a man who has stood in the relation
of a contributor to Mr. Ward as editor, but will
regret his abandonment of the position which he
carved out for himself—Yours, .Journalist.

A well-known Western district (Vie.)
teetotal squatter took a considerable rise out of
two bullock-drivers the other day. The bullockies
had drawn up beside an unlocked gate leading
into one of the squatter’s best grass paddocks, and
were preparing to camp, when the jovial land-
owner drove up en route, for home. With rustic
politeness (aided perhaps by the pangs of consci-
ence) one of the ox-conductors stepped up and
opened the gate, and after the conveyance had
driven through attempted to shut it again, when
lie was paralysed by the occupant of the buggy
turning round in his seat and addressing him
thusly —“ Thank you, my man; never mind
shutting it. I know very well that you’ll turn
your bullocks in as soon as I am out of sight, so
you may as well save yourself any unnecessary
trouble. Good-bye.”
Oh, Granny, Granny I Fie for shame!
It grievei us much to see
You robbing gentlemen of fame,
Omitting their J.P.
From any kind of beastly pride
We’re singularly free ;
But still we will not be denied
Our mystical J.P.
We would not murmur if you dropped
Our paltry LL.D.,
But from our name must not be lopped
Our dearly-loved J.P.
We’re not particular about
Our Ph.D. degree,
But, no, we cannot live without
Our glorious J.P.
The world is welcome to our purse
(Containing one and three),
But one and all will earn our curse,
Ignoring our J.P.
And when to earth we say farewell,
Our last request will be,
Oh, let our tombstone proudly tell,
In life we were J.P.
The Editor’s Wail.
(JfoK The Bulletin.)
Came out of his “ den ” the poor Bogalong editor.
The beer on his whiskers was heavy and chill;
He had just had a row with a bloodthirsty
creditor,
Who had (with a bulldog) come after his bill.
He was haggard, unwashed, his pants wanted re-
seating,
And he had but one answer to everyone’s greeting,
Which was, between drinks, to keep sadly re-
peating,
“ O, write us a ‘leader,’ or give us a par!”
For news was as scarce as are ice-creams in winter,
And sad in his heart was the editor man;
Tormented all day he had been by his printer,
Till at last, with a curse, from the office he ran.
With a frenzied despair he had asked the school-
master,
The plugs in the bar-room, the Methodist pastor,
To help him along, and, to save from disaster,
To write him a “leader,”or give him a par ” !
For no one had died, and none had got married,
None had been fined for unregistered dogs ;
None had eloped, no one had been carried
“ Drunk and disorderly,” off to the “ logs ” —
No horses been stolen, no cattle impounded,
Not even a man for his debts had compounded,
And sadly the editor’s voice thus resounded —
“ O, write us a ‘ leader,’ or give us a ‘ par ’ !”
But “pars” he got none from the plugs or the
pastor,
They only just “ filled ’em up ” at his expense ;
Nor did he get anything from the schoolmaster,
Who streaked away homeward across the back-
fence.
So at last, in despair, this humble depender
On other folks’ labours went off on a “ bender,"
In the wild course of which he oft uttered a tender
“ 0, write us a ‘ leader,’ or give us a ‘ par ’!”
The Bogalong paper that night did not come out.
For printer and “ devil ” as one man agreed
That “ the boss had no time to get all ttat rum
out,”
And so they both cheerfully went forth and
“ spreed.”
And as they imbibed with increasing devotion,
And drank as if draining the Indian Ocean,
They murmured at times, with a beery emotion,
“ 0! write him a ‘leader,’or give him a ‘ par’!”
The Bogalong editor’s not taken warning
By this baleful result and the loss of much pelf,
Still he goes onward, persistently scorning
The broadest of hints to go write for himself ;
If transported he be to the meadows Elysian _
He will ask the first angel who crosses his vision,
Or the demon with whom he comes first in collision,
To “ write him a ‘leader,’ or give him a ‘ par ’!”
A.C.

A Ballad of Bungaloo.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We sing a lay, a lay that’s true,
About the township Bungaloo,
A many miles away;
Two hundred it may be, or three,
Or even four, the fact is we
Cannot exactly say.
It’s not the most exciting place,
And Bungalooites are a race
Exceptionally meek;
However strange it may appear,
They’re rarely longer “ on the beer ”
Than seven days a week.
The sole excitement to be found
Is when the Sessions-day comes round
For nothing seems to fetch
The simple mind of Bungaloo
So much as when a pal or two
Is sent to “do a stretch.”
Last Sessions there a case occurred,
Of which you probably have heard ;
’Twas that of Matthew Reece,
Air undertaker, he, by trade,
Who only recently was made
A Justice of the Peace.
Idle crime of winch he stood accused
Was that he, Matthew Ileece, had used
The local butcher’s name
On cheques for various amounts;
He also had to answer counts
Of uttering the same.
The charge created great surprise,
For Matthew, in his neighbours’ eyes,
Was looked upon as one
Who might employ his leisure skill
In drawing up a bogus bill
In simple, childish fun ;
Bat that he should have forged a cheque,
And so become a social wreck,
Was more than they could stand :
The moral mind of Bungaloo
Would very properly eschew
A trick so underhand.
And so when Ileece’s case began,
The natives gather to a man,
And to the court repair ;
From north, south, cast and west they conic,
In various degrees of rum
Of fragrance rich and rare.
To Mr. Perry Wigg, Q.C.,
They listen eagerly as he
Expounds each legal clause ;
At ev’ry tolling point he makes
The courthouse literally shakes
With hiecough-y applause.
The learned Counsel argued long,
Their eloquence was hot and strong,
_ Their arguments were deep ;
Each minute the excitement grew,
Till someone drew attention to
His Honor fast asleep.
An ink-pot, dexterously flung,
Awakened Air. Jratice Bung,
(Indeed it was no less).
Who rubbed his sleepy eyes aud cried;
“Oh, leave the bally hoots outside
And bring a 8.-and-S. !”
When he to consciousness returned,
Twas hoped the Court would be adjourned
(The clock was striking two) ;
But “ ro,” remarked the learned judge,
“ From this here bench I will not bu lge
Until the case is through.”
Addressing Mr. Perry Wigg,
He roared : “ You really are as big
A fool as you appear ;
I’m sick of this infernal din,
A clearer case was never in
The whole of my career.
“ The very fact that Matthew Reece
Is proved a Justice of the Peace
In this here one-horse place
Appeals to intellects most dense;
He ne’er committed the offence
He’s charged with in this case.
“ Experience of bush J’sP.
Has long made palpable to me
That barely one in ten
(Statistics prove that I’m correct)
Is gifted with the intellect
To engineer a pen.
“ It should be, therefore, very plain
That if he hasn’t got the brain
■ To write his own—Great Scott !
That he should write a name not his
Is an absurd hypothesis,
In fact, is bally rot I”
Prisoner discharged accordingly.
Sydney, 10th May, 1890.

weather, says someone, So it is, say we, but why

Pastor Addis, Charles Strong’s colleague
at the Australian Church, Melbourne, writes that
there never were any real miracles. “For the
bodily resurrection of Jesus there is no evidence
worthy of the name. One disciple believed in an
ecstatic moment that he had seen the risen Lord.
A whole congregation of 500 persons at once felt
the same electric thrill .pass through them, but the
appearance was a phantom, ”
“Bob” Watson, one of the prospectors
who boro the heat and burden of the day in open-
ing up North Queensland, and after whom Wat-
sonville was named, is dead. In his last trip,
through some wild country, he was so reduced by
illness and want of proper nourishment that he
had to be lifted on and off his horse. Though six
feet high and strongly-made, he weighed at the
end of the journey just over eight stone. He was
a splendid bushman and a man who never turned
his back either on enemy or friend.
Special attention is directed to the
“Angola” Spectacles just patented and now
manufactured by Mr, L. Shragcr, of 111 Macquarie*
place. The “ Angola ” don’t require branches and
sit with equal firmness on shapely and deformed
probosci, and arc made, of “ real pebbles ”
mounted with gold, silver and tortoise-shell.
Another speciality is Mr. Shrager’s “Monograms,”
a new device for stamping linen, embroidery,
note-paper, &c., which took prizes at the Mel-
bourne Exhibition. The inventive rnanufacturer’s
prices arc said to be lower than those of any other
house.
The late “Pat” Keighran was once at a
very up-country race-meeting when the rider of
one of the horses in the principal handicap found
himself above the required weight, and adjusted
matters by pulling off his bluchers and riding in
his socks. Just, before the start “ Pat ” met the
owner of the animal, and remarked that he now
considered the race a certainty for him. “ How’s
that?” asked the gratified owner. “Why,” re-
turned “Pat,” “that horse must be a glutton if
he doesn’t make a record in his attempts to get
away from those socks. ”
“ Tommy ” Danger, the silent member
for the Namoi, N.S.W., was, in the old days, an
inveterate practical joker. On one occasion he
took some friends to see him play off a lark on a
young fellow who occupied a hut on an out-
statiou. This young fellow, who, by the way,
was rather “balmy” in the upper story, had
rigged up his bunk on some sheets of bark on the
beams overhead, and when “Tommy,” with his
accomplices standing off to see the fun, approached
the door and shouted to the occupant to open, as
he was so-and-so, mentioning a well-known bush-
ranger of the day, “ Balmy,” from his perch, sent
the first barrel of a duck-gun point-blank at the
frail door, and only “ Tommy’s” terrified shrieks
as to who he was prevented the second barrel fol-
lowing. Mr. Hangar does not tell this now
amongst his list of successful practical jokes.

A gentleman visited the Royal Water-
Colour Institute (London) the other day and
bought pictures to the value of £3OOO. The fel-
lows whose pictures were not selected said he
must be mad; and when his cheque was pre-
sented it was discovered that he really was.
Minister Dow solemnly denies the truth
of the charge of conspiracy brought against him as
a director of the Premier Permanent Building
Society. Pending particularly clear proof to the
contrary, the public are quite ready to accept his
denial.

read Thk Bulletin’s article this week on the
“ Poor Heathen.'’

unearths an extraordinary and inexplicable refer-
ence by Macaulay, in his famous Copyright Speech,
year iB4l, to “Victoria and Sydney,” whereas
the name of Victoria, for Port Phillip, was ap-
parently not then thought of. David Blair
scratches Ids head. Possibly a slip of the pen, or
speech, by Macaulay, suggested the name “Vic-
toria.”

“Our Boys ” have made a large commencement
by beating what practically amounted to an; Ab-Kiigland
Lie-ven in one innings. T.ie daily papeis, in accordance
with contemptible precedent, discovered that this victory
—which would have been a defeat if Murdoch had called
“ heads” instead of “ tails,” or >:ice term— was a “ splen-
did performance.” By this lime Warwicks..ire has pro-
bably been knocked into a cocked hut, and “another
splendid performance ” stands to the credit of our cham-
pions. If we give five minutes' thought to the Shef-
field Park match we discover nothing (or certain,
except that the wicket for the Britishers’ first inn-
ings must have been abominable. -Ten batsmen got
rid of for seven runs reads like sufficient cause for the
kangaroo to wag his tail, until we renumber that two
Australians (including Murdoch) had just been bowled for
one run on that same wicket. Of the first day’s play all
we know is that the turf was reported to be in first-class
condition, anil that Murdoch and Go. could make no more
t han 190 runs in nearly six hours. Living them every
credit for winning, Tire Bulletin maintains that our
daily Brass would have pooh-poohed an Australian defeat
under such conditions as those that gave the Britishers
no show.

* # «
.Sydney Amateur Athletic Club brought off the
s’c .ml of their club contests on 7lh May, when William
Faber and John Fuller met “ to a finish ” for £100 purse,
.175 to winner, and £25 to loser. Maher is of the Billy
Murphy build, only more so, as he lacks the muscle which
Skinny Murphy shows. His legs would disfigure a poor
turkey, and his breastbone would put to the blush the
proverbial hoarding-house fowl, but he gets there all the
same, and knocked Fuller senseless in the twenty-fourth
round. Fuller, whom Tire Billetix lias ever looked upon
as an overrated man, never struck a blow worthy of the
name, and Maher,, who is a very clever boxer, hit him
when and where he liked after the first few rounds. The
winner, who hails from Adelaide, is now anxious for a
match with Burge, but the writer does not think he is
“in it” with “Ironhark Jimmy,” who would just be
thinking it was time to commence fighting, were he to re-
ceive the amount of punishment which knocked Fuller
out. Maher is a scientific boxer and a hard hitter, but his
weight and physique will not. in the writer's opinion,
permit of his lasting to a finish with the superhuman
Burge.

Carroll-M'Auliffe fight to London Storting Like by re-

that a Californian fight has been so reported in London.
The contest, a forty-seven-round one, is said to have been
one of the best ever witnessed. Carroll, who was de-
feated, has since knocked out “Billy” Smith, the Aus-
tralian middle-weight, in It rounds.

We learnt lately by cable that J. E. Savill,
owner of Ringmaster, does not like the English system of
training ; an ironical comment on his opinion is the cable
which states that Mat. O’Shanassy’s Chicago, in his care,
has broken down badly. Plutarch has broken a vessel,
Narellan is still growing, Kirkham has got no foot, and
Ring-master’s form is known. So Australia’s present show-
on the British turf looks small.

in St. Jacobs oil appears to He in its superior pene-

St. Jacobs oil is peculiar to itself. It is made

principles. St. Jacobs oil is an outward applica-

EXPERTS AND CRYSTALS.
Have you ever examined critically the crystals
rued in the bezels of the Waterbury Watches ? If
not, then this will suggest so doing at your first
opportunity. Each of the glasses used undergoes
a minute and careful scrutiny in the hands of an
expert—an expert whose precision and untiring
fidelity appears almost marvellous. Crystals are
often rejected whose defects are so slight as to
escape not only the ordinary eye, but even the
vision of foremen and trained employes in other-
branches. Yet, upon taking one of them to the
expert, that person will invariably point out the
fault, and do it, withal, so quickly that one would
almost believe the spot to have been marked for
identification. The testing of crystals, rigid as it
is, is but a sample of the method and care preva-
lent in every branch of the Waterbury Watch
Co.’s factory. Precision is the keystone to their
success; and therein lies the secret of the wonder-
ful results obtained in the time-keeping qualities
of their marvellously reliable watches, produced
at a nominal cost. The people of Great Britain
and of the PTnited States are eminently practical,
while those of the Australasian colonies are nowise
behind them in this particular, and the continued
flow of acknowledgements received from them
could not be wrung forth by misrepresentation,
nor sustained by anything else than the most
deservedly practical worth.

A new half-penny weekly, to be edited by
Archibald Grove, of the successful New .Review,
is the latest London project.
The summarised results of a series of in-
quiries made as to the operation of the death-
penalty, both in America and in the principal
European countries, are issued in leaflet form by
the Howard Association. The returns, covering
ten years, prove not merely the extreme un-
certainty which marks the inflation of capital
punishment, but shows that uncertainty "greatly
weakens or nullifies its deterrent character. ’’ It
is curious to reflect that Finland— that remarkable
country now struggling to preserve its inde-
pendence - had abolished capital punishment in,
and has had no execution since, lb2o. It is also
significant to remark that “murder has not in-
creased during these sixty-four years/’ In these
parts of the United States where the death penalty
is still in vogue, the record is gloomy indeed.
A miserable specimen of (I. A. Sala’s
philosophy appears in the A uers. The garrulous
old fat gentleman reckons up the position of the-
slop-trade hands, at the East-end of London, as
beyond the reach of human remedy : “ Alas I poor
people; how are they to organise? and against
whom? and what would be, in the end, the result
of organisation against the Inevitable- which in-
evitable I tike to be the determination of the
public to have an ample supply, of cheap
ready-made clothing, the production of which
means semi-starvation wages?” Heavy feeding
has impaired Sala’s men tal vision, and the generous
liquors he delights to' tattle about have dulled his
understanding. He snuffles like a parson to whom
the existing state of affairs is always the result of
the great “ Inevitable. ’ Sala's paragraph re-
minds one of a sermon upon the beauties of con-
tentment in every department of life to which the
Loard has called us. It was written for the
Akgl'h, and ought to conclude with the Doxology.
