In Siberia, 1890.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
They said ; “ The man must go.”
A weary knot of fainting women-folk,
Faint crushed, and sinking ’neath their load of
woe,
Goaded with ruthless taunt or cruel blow,
Life’s hopes all strangled by Oppression’s yoke,
What power lay in these faces set and wan ?
Yet list-—from lip to lip the whisper ran,
Ran with a gathering murmur strong and low ;
“ The man shall go. ”
What was he? Nothing—Might’s dark instru-
ment
For woe—no more. But this to them meant all,
These strong-souled women-rnartyrs who had lent
Their lives, their youth, their cherished loves—all
spent
In effort to make Might’s chief stronghold fall.
He, the embodied emblem of brute force,
Had goaded them to madness ; but one course
Lay open ; they, with hatred long inpent,
Decreed his banishment.
What roused them ? Only this: One bitter morn
A tender woman dragged from pallet-bed ;
Dragged by rough soldier hands, and stripped and
torn
With ribald soldier jest and brutal scorn.
What matter ? There had been no warm blood
shed,
No nameless outrage. What were woman’s cries?
Sounds easy silenced. Yet the threats arise ;
Better for them if they had ne’er been born.
We pill avenge that morn.
The means ? Great God ! At hand
They found them. In their vitals. Only one
Last pang their tyrants grudged them : Death*
whose wand
Brings rest xxnto the restless ; Death’s cold hand
They must not woo. For Death tells tales, and
none
Will deem the murdered happy. So these same
Long-toi’tured women swore to sear the name
Of their foul tyrant with the murder-brand —
“Wc starve.'"’ So pledged the baxxd.
The Hunger-strike—the bond-in-death,
The sacrament of gasping bx-eath,
Of vulture-vitals, burning brain,
Dry lips from which the life-drops drain,
Hot eyeballs in the sockets sunk,
Parched skin upon the bare bones shrunk,
A shrivelled frame, grown gaunt and grim,
A glazing eye, grown weak and dim,
A tottering step, a pulse on fire,
A thi’obbing, shudd’ring, fierce desire
For meat, bread, blood—like pangs to these
Have brought wild men upon their knees,
Taught gentle souls to seize the knife
And madly, with a brother’s life,
Make loathsome nxeal —nay, rumours speak
Of tender mothers, waste and weak,
With hearts so starved that, dried and dead
To Imman sense, they took for bread
The nurseling at their withered breast—
Twere sacrilege to speak the rest.
Yet these faint women, strong of soul,
Turned dumbly from the daily dole
Pressed on them by a tyrant’s care—
Vile food, in sooth, ye.t tempting fare
To starving stomachs—
Leaden hours
Drag blindly, marking off the powers
Of martyr-spirits ; hours are days,
Whose throbbing, quivering pulses craze
The maddened brain ; and days are weeks,
Yet still the hollowed eye and cheeks ;
The burnt, dried skin alone bespeaks
Th e secret anguish ;
Till at last
The piercing agony is passed,
Till wrenching, gnawing throe grows numb,
Till shrinking, wailing nerve is dumb,
Till over-balanced thought and brain
Live o’er the happy past again.
Across the thin and freezing air
Float Mem’ry’s visions, sweet and fair.
Ere Hope had paled before Despair.
Vision of gleaming, courtly ball
Where lightsome foot and laughter fall
.Adown the glamour of the hall;
Of princely lover, on whose tongue
In tender, ’passioned rhythm rung
The old, old story, ever-young ;
Of stately home on Tartar plain,
And fleeting steeds with tossing mane,
Foaming, the while they champ the chain ;
Of humbler home, where mother’s love,
Brooding and tender as a dove,
Prays to the guardian saints above ;
Of student life, with earnest aim,
Wherinto, sudden, leaps tlxe flame
Whex-eby the patriot signs his name;
Of midnight meetings, secret, still,
Where hearts were consecrate, and will,
To save their country, die, or kill.
This was their past—so near, so near,
What echo came therefrom, what cheer ?
W hat power had dragged them thence—and here ?
Here ?,—God ! —The pangs revive, the light
Of that dread present blinds their sight—
Ah, well, the end is well-nigh won,
Death scarce can tarry :
Nay, ’tis done.
A sudden whisper steals along,
Thrilling with subtle joy that throng :
“ The man doth go ; our task is o’er.”
It lasted eighteen days—-no more;
But eighteen days when bite nor sup
Was tasted ; so power yielded up
Her tyranny to Weakness.
Ay,
Yon deem their patience folly, say
Their end was small and worthless? You
Have had a sister pure and true,
A mother, music-toned and mild,
Or, dearer still, a daughter-child ?
These, too, had dear ones left to toil,
With Freedom’s seed on Russian soil ;
Sisters, whose hand had clasped their hand,
Young daughters trained to join the band
Of Truth’s Iconoclasts ; some day
They, too, must tread the frozen way,
Must know Siberia’s prison gloom,
Languish within a prison tomb
’Mid sickening horrors, bear each pang
In patient dignity, the fang
Of dirt and hunger, or the low
Brute word and jest—but outrage—No ?
For this, for them, was borne that woe,
For them that day was won.
Won? Lo,
Fiend subtleties are cruel; go
To lower hell for polished craft
As that who barbed the poisoned shaft
Borne with the words, “Ye are deceived !”
At first the truth was disbelieved ;
Then when the hated form was seen
They set their teeth and vowed.
Again—
The fight was started—silent pain
’Gainst violent power; for eight long days
This second struggle; then the word
Is given “he shall go.” They heard
And trembled. Was it true ? In sooth
Their tyrants knew not simple truth,
But lied, to their damnation.
Now
War to the end ! Weak nerves are steel
Whetted to pierce a tyrant heel;
And ruling Might wears iron bands
To nerve the grip of its fierce hands ;
Never was sadder combat known
Than this, where silence or deep moan
Met reckless curse from hearts of stone.
Each vowed to win ; and one had Power,
And one had Patience. Whose the hour
Of victory ? Dear pity ! Here
Were women, wasted to the mere
Semblance of life and sworn to die.
While there—-were men with bloodshot eye
And raging fury, sworn to make
These pale ghosts live; so racking pain
Of death was met by racking pain
Of enforced life. The hideous tale
No pen dare word in full. Men pale
At thought of all that underlies
The record. Dying women’s cries ;
Women who vainly courted death
While brutal hands fanned back their breath.
The end de’ays : yet twenty-one
Long nightmare days are come and gone—
Days spent in Hell. But two alone
Of all those women now can stand
On tottering feet; and 10, their grand
Self-martyrdom but comes to this —
That they are mastered. Serpent hiss
Is not more curdling than that thought,
Their deadly purpose still unwrought,
Their suffering mocked at! Of those twain
One plans a deed ; her eager brain
Sees one road to salvation, through
A victim’s blood. Strong heart and true
She marks herself for sacrifice.
“ One death may save the rest,” she cries,
“ And win the goal.” Then silently
She lifts her burden —patiently
To climb her Calvary. A word
Was brought the Governor, which stirred
His eagerness—He? Wanted? Well,
This rebel must have news to tell
Of late submission; and in haste
He stands before that woman, waste
In limb and visage, strong in glance;
He, low and fleshly, waits parlance
And dreams of chains fresh riveted.
She struck him !
Christ when gibbeted
On cross had not a purer aim
Than this poor woman’s heart could claim
The while she dealt that blow. She dealt
Her life with it, and only felt
Her people’s freedom looming in
The distant future ; that to win
She gave herself to death.
But Death were merciful, and wolves
Know naught of mercy: she should have her
meed ;
She courted all the martyr’s fate involves,
Then let her gather up her high resolves,
For she should win her martyrdom indeed ;
One hundred lashes —neither more nor less ;
One hundred lashes. There is no redress;
Rebellion from all human claims absolves,
All human ties dissolves.
One hundred lashes ! She who faced the grave,
Before this torture feels her spirits shrink.
One hundred lashes. Stoutest heart and brave
Might faint appalled—and there is none to save
This wasted woman, trembling on the brink
Of death’s dark valley. With her feeble frame
The hardened prison-surgeon brands the name
Of murder on the sentence ; Let him rave!
Vengeance its orders gave.
• • • • •
Hush ! hush ! It is the murder day,
See Holy Russia armed to slay
One feeble woman—in that hour
Did no chill spectre haunt the Power
Pointing with finger gaunt its doom ?
The murder-day. From living tomb
She passes with her tottering gait—
Passes from out the prison gate—
To that sole freedom men afford.
Men ! those who saw that sight abhorred,
The brawny arms, the knotted thong,
The sullen, low-browed, stolid throng,
The pale, weak victim with a light
Upon heFbrow—who saw that sight
And raised no weapon, raised no cry,
They lost their manhood—Pass them by.
She stands alone amid that crowd,
Alone, untended, calm and proud ;
An iron patience in her face,
And in her smile a bitter grace—
The smile of one who seeks in vain
Some tender pity for her pain.
’Twas her Gethsemane, and 10,
The woman-Christ there learnt to know
The loneliness of human woe.
The scourge is raised, her flesh is bare,
Her woman’s flesh, so soft and fair,
A hissing sound flies through the air,
A flashing thought flies through her brain
E’en while awaiting that wild pain ;
The memory of tones of love,
Of lingering sweet caress, unwove
With tender word Good God ! love’s kiss
Was prelude to such shame as this,
The form that thrilled beneath his touch
Must blench and quiver in the clutch
Of fiends ; the heart that throbbed
One shriek
On shrinking air—thus tyrants wreak
Vengeance upon the helpless ; blood
Spurts on the ground, a clotted flood.
That quivering, tender woman’s back
The sinewy seourgers hew and hack,
Leave vivid scars in tangled mesh,
Slice living strips of dripping flesh;
The scourge’s sick’ning monotone
Draws forth at times a shuddering moan.
Mother of God ! What pangs are these ?
Once, in her far-off days of ease,
She suffered like; e’en then as now,
Blanched, wrenched and tortured with each throe,
She dreamed that Earth was Hell below.
But, ah ! th’ anirelic after-calm
Falling in tender life-long balm,
The brooding joy, the half-numb sense
Of suffering’s holiest recompense,
When pulse and nerve and entrail torn
Throb to the words ; A child is born.
And these were infants once ! A wild
Hope seizes on her—through the child—
To rouse the man and loose his fangs
By calling on his mother’s pangs.
The hope, scarce born, dies in her breast
These had no mothers. Savage beasts
Suckled them into fierceness ; vain
To ask their pity for her pain,
She can but bear it -sudden came,
Amid the torture and the shame,
A gladsome dream; each maddening blow
That tore her flesh was as the throe
Of all the nations’ labour ; she
Had won a fresh maternity,
That larger motherhood, when Earth
1 folds revel over Freedom’s birth
And cradles giants, born to tear
The tiger-tyrant from his lair
And fling him down the ravine ; yea,
Anguish like her’s proclaimed the day
When despots pale on reeling throne
And despotism’s heirs atone
Unto their victims’ childi’en ; when
Women bring forth, not slaves, but men.
So be it. Let them wreak their worst;
Nor piteous sob nor cry should burst
From her set lips; her ebbing life,
Mastered in the unequal strife,
Net shares the world’s great travail and
Peoples with freemen all the land ;
Ay, let them do their worst.
She kept
Her patient silence; sickness crept
On strong-limbed men, all used to deed
Of horror ; scourge-thongs reek and bleed
The while they flay her —still no sign,
No murmur, e’en dull brutes repine
And weary, though they dare not shirk,
Meting out, stroke by stroke, their work.
’Tis o'er.
One hundred lashes. ’Mid the gore
There lies an undistinguished mass—
N ot woman, not yet corpse, alas !
They take it up, a nameless thing
W ith so much life, just flickering,
As knows of some all-mastering pain;
Knows, too, of some dim purpose, lain
Aside, but cherished, not yet won
But suffered for, and therefore done.
Two days the soul clung, by a thread,
Unto that battered mass—then fled
To Freedom.
When her gaolers carao
To view their work, a newer shame
Awaited them : within that cell
Three other corpses lay, to tell
< )f woe past bearing.
And beyond,
In the men’s quarters, there the bond
Of death clung, strong and pitiful,
While gaoler hands, less merciful,
Tortured those back to life who sought
The ease that Death’s own torture brought.
And this was all ? Poor fools, to risk so much
And gain so little. Even so, wise friend,
’Tis fools like these redeem the world, not such
As thou and I who bargain over-much
And deem reward an all-sufficient end.
These pass. Their seed remains; its branch and
root
Are spreading outwards : the slow ripening fruit
Shall feed the nations, but at tyrant’s touch
Breathe poison. Let them crouch.
Unto what end ? Behold the spectre rise,
Crimson and gaunt, from where the ground is
stained
With human blood ! It comes, with haunting eyes,
The despot sees it from his throne and cries
In pallid fear for that his power has waned.
He shrinks behind his shields, the dungeon grim,
The scourge, the rack, but naught availeth him.
On, on, with rushing wing, the spectre flies,
While lightnings fork the skies.
On, on, and lo ! its crimson flag unfurled,
Round which the nations gather with a shout
Whereof the mighty echoes shake the world ;
Thrones totter, superstition’s priests are hurled
Adown their altar-steps, and all about
The peoples falls a halo of white light.
Unto what end ? From out the coming night
Bid forth thy nameless ghost, to view the world
Where laugh thy children’s children.
ROSE DE BOHEME.
Fifty-six men were killed during the con-
struction of the Forth Bridge. An average of £7O!
was paid to each of the bereaved families.
It was Stipendiary Call, of Melbourne, who
expressed great surprise when a police constable
told him he had been two months in the force and
had not yet got a watch.
Dear Bulletin, —In a certain western
township a fair but frail young woman was once
fined £5 for being drunk arid disorderly and using
obscene language. A gentleman in court felt great
pity for the poor girl, and, speaking “financially ”
to a few others, said, “She’s a good girl, is
Emily ; pity for her to go to jail—; just before the
races, too.” The gentleman is now a J.P. ; but
that’s nothing. They get there just the same.—
Bar Blox.
Idle Pall Mall Gazette is a conspicuous
instance of a bungled newspaper. Greenwood
worked it up in support of Disraeli, but when
Dizzy fell, the proprietors called upon Greenwood
to face right about, which he gallantly refused to
do, wherefore Morley made a mull of the editor-
ship, and then Stead came in. He has failed, too.
The paper is now bottom side up. The old London
Morning Chronicle went bung because the
proprietors sold it to Napoleon 111. And why
has the Times bust up ?
Melbourne Age takes to pieces the political
mechanism of Sir Henry Parke's in relation to the
Federation question : “ Altogether, in the simple
matter of bringing forward these really formal
resolutions before the Parliament of N.S. Wales,
Sir Henry Parkes has placed himself in the worst
of lights, He has shown himself incapable of
forgetting personal animosities in face of a great
national demand on his patriotism.. He has
irritated an important party in N. S. Wale*, and
has gone out of his way to stir up intercolonial
bad feeling; and, in acting thus, he deliberately
endangers the Federal scheme."
Six harmless and decrepit old lunatics
have been turned adrift by the New Zealand
Government. Their contention is that the paupers
are suffering from “ senile decay,” that forty other
patients in the Avondale Asylum are similarly
afflicted, and that there is no room for them.
Accordingly, they were driven to the Charitable
Aid Board offices and dumped down on the foot-
path. Refused admittance, they were transferred
to_ the Police Court, jabbering, sniggering and
drivelling They werethen remanded and locked up.
Eventually they were taken care ofrby the Salva-
tion Army—which now seems to be&bout the only
church game to publicly extend its hand to the
shabby man down on his luck or just out of gaol.

CADBURY'S COCOA is closely allied to
milk in the large proportion of flesh-forming and I
strength-sustaining elements that it contains. It is pre-
pared on the principle of excluding the superabundance
of fatty indigestible matter with which Cocoa abounds

“Kuk-kuk-kuk-kuk-Crawshay-not
Kuk-kuk-Crotchet.”
Dear Bulletin, —Your delightful narrative of
“A Queensland Whist Party” revives in my
mind some personal knowledge of that same Craw-
shay, whose bad whist evoked classical language
from a Queensland Minister of the Crown on the
occasion you tell of. Crawshay has since “ struck
it hot,’’and he is welcome to his luck, for he was
a whole-souled, devil-may-care fellow. He seems
to have taken the objurgations of the Minister in
good part, which fact can only be accounted for to
my mind by the supposition that Crawshay re-
spected the other’s age—for he was a bit hot-
tempered. When I knew him—about 1873 —he
had purchased Murillo Station (or some interest
in it) from Joshua Peter Bell, and was going the
pace on remittances from his uncle in Wales—
“Crawshay, the Iron King.” Now our particular
Crawshay is himself the “Iron King,” hav-
ing succeeded to his uncle’s pile. “ Why
don’t you get married ?” I used to say to
Crawshay at that time. “Not such a kok-
kok-kok-kok-ko-koufounded idiot ! ” he would
answer ; “ Pm not gug-gug-gug-going to bo kuk-
kuk-eut off with a shilling.” (You will perceive
that Crawshay had an orchestral impediment in
his speech.) It appears that Uncle Crawshay’s son
had married against his father’s wishes and been
cut off as aforesaid, while our Crawshay had been
publicly made heir and used to get remittances in
regular lumps of £5OO at a time. To see Crawshay
and a mad Scotchman who used to chaw glass in
his Bacchanalian moods, get on the burst together
in Dalby township was a complete phantasmagoria
in itself. Crawshay was deasly fond of horseflesh.
He could ride anything and loved to master a
buckjumper. For myself, I never could get a sure
seat on any native horse, and often has Crawshay
yelled out to me, “ Dud-dud-don’t stick
your knees into the pup-pup-pup-pad; ride by
bub-bub-bub-bub-balanoe, like I do.” But I
never could. He loved a horse that was as un-
even and rapid in its movements'as he was in his
speech. This was his joke:—“l’m a beb-beb-
heb-beggar to stu-tut-tut-tutter, and it’s a beb-
heb-beggar to bub-bub-buck!” I was present
when he purchased that moke. It was a rough,
third-rate station-horse —an ornery thirty-bob
animal. It had been turned out to grass because
it was no good, and was for six months on Jim-
hour station “filling out.'’ One day, when
Crawshay happened to be there, and some of his
horses were being run in for him, this scrubber
was yarded with them. It was “flash,” and it
looked well with its bush-fed coat on. A black-
fellow saddled it in order to ride out for some
other wanted horses. The darkey was no
sooner in the saddle than he was out of it.
Crawshay rushed excitedly into the yard, caught
the horse, jumped on its back, and was
indantcr landed on his own. Foaming with ex-
citement and a sense of injury, he “ splodged ”
out both hands theatrically, and yelled (at nobody
in particular) ; “Th-th-th-that wasn’t fair ; was it,
now? I wasn’t on the beb-beb-beb-beb-beggar’s
back at all. Bet-bet-bet-bet bet-bet you fif-fif-fifty
pup-pounds I ride him down quiet.” Nobody
objected. The horse being again caught, and held
by one man while another placed his hands over
the animal’s eyes, Crawshay mounted, and, being
squarely in the saddle, sang out :“ Leggo !” A
scattering of onlookers and an excited circus per-
formance followed. “ No. 19,” bucking all round
the yard, and Crawshay sitting delightedly in his
jerky seat, too transported to stutter even in his
thinking. After a spell of round-the-ring practice,
he signalled for the panels to be opened, and, like
a bolt from a bow, “No. 19 ” sped with his rider
out upon the Jimbour plains. Then a smart thing
was done. A “knowing one” offered the man
who owned “ No. 19 ” a fiver for the horse. It was
joyfully accepted, the beast not being considered
worth “a note.” An hour elapsed and Craw-
shay returned to the house with “No. 19” con-
quered and reeking with lather. Crawshay was
beaming with elation. “ Puf-fuf-finest horse ever
I rode,” he said; “who owns it?” He was told
that a gentlemen had only just bought it from the'
late owner. Went Crawshay to the “knowing
one ’’ and spake : “ Gug-gug-gug-gug-give yer £lO
for him.” “ Don’t care about selling him ; fancy
him myself; I have an idea of his pedigree, ” &c., &c.
The usual gag followed—the “knowing one”
freezing and Crawshay getting hotter at every
impediment against his purchase. It ended in the
“knowing one” reluctantly parting with his £5
purchase for £64 and a horse to boot !
Crawshay did not shine in society and small
talk. I remember well how he distinguished him-
self at Joshua Peter Bell’s table one evening.
Several visitors were at the station. There were
seated at the dining-table, Mr. and Mrs, J. P,
Bell, Miss C , the then Dalby parson (Mably
or Moberley ; I never could retain hold of the
calendar of the saints), Bob Gray (now one of the
Queensland Railway Commissioners), “Cumkillen-
bar ” Moffat, Crawshay, a friend of his whose
name I forget, and myself. Miss 0 , a bright,
intellectual, and sprightly lady, told a gossipy
anecdote, then current, about some Royal
princess’s marriage. (I am equally loose in my
memory about Royalties.) The story was that
the English princess had been asked what present
she would like to receive from Emperor
William (now in Limbo), of Germany, and
that she replied ; “ Send me Bismarck’s
head on a charger.” Now, Crawshay had
been making himself agreeable to Miss C ,
and she being of the intellectual order of mental
architecture, he doubtless desired to display as
much of his erudition as he could at impromptu
call. So when Miss C had concluded the yarn
he guffawed ; “ Head on a eha-cha-cha-charger !
Hoo-hoo-hoo-hoo-ha ! That’s good—very gug-
gug-good ! Let’s see. It was kuk kuk-kuk-kuk-
King John who first said that, wasn’t it ?” Soon
after that Crawshay passed out of my acquaint-
anceship, and I had forgotten him till reading the
whist story in The Bulletin. He is a millionaire
by heredity, whereas I am only (whether by
heredity or not) —
THE DIPSOMANIAC.
The Broken Hill Proprietary Silver Mine
pays a monthly dividend equalling £40,000.
Nearly six-eighths of this goes into Adelaide.
F. H. Pollock writes sensibly to the Mel-
bourne Press on the stage-children question. He
shows the hollow humbug of the thing, for, while
Violet Ashton is prosecuted for acting intelligently
in “A Man’s Shadow,” the Melbourne streets
abound towards midnight with girls and boys
in rags selling matches, &c., but Constables
Boodlecum and Poodlecum take no heed what-
ever. But then these kids ain’t on the ’orrid
stage. The poor youngsters, without shoes or
stockings, are not swept into the State schools, be-
cause they off end “respectable” parents, the object
of the Education Act being to give gratis instruc-
tion only to the progeny of those able to pay for it.
The Latest Atrocity.
- An Irishman has recently been sentenced (in Ireland,
of course) to three months’ imprisonment for winking at a
pig. A policeman swore he saw the prisoner distinctly
winking at a certain pig belonging to a boycotted man
who was trying to sell the animal. The wink was intended
to prevent the porker’s sale.
Hip, hip, hooray ! with three times three
For England’s mighty Queen,
The greatest monarch, barring none,
The world has ever seen ;
All round the globe her laws prevail,
Although it is so big,
In Ireland, too, they never fail
Where recently they sent to gaol
A Parnellite (ye traitors quail!)
For winking at a pig.
She’s Empress of the golden East
And mistress of the seas,
No potentates or Emperors
Dare at “Her Gracious ’’ sneeze ;
Her latest triumph, though it may
At first appear—what shall we say?—
A leetle infra dig,
Has given the Parnellites a knock !
They’ll never more her feelings shock
By winking at a pig !
When Tory swells for awful crimes
Are wanted —don’t you know—
A kindred feeling makes her kind
And so she lets them go !
Her old Imperial heart is stirred,
She treats them well, upon my word !
Each erring Tory sprig ;
But when a Paddy’s eyelid drops
Her man in blue the rascal cops
For winking at a pig.
Times’ Pig-got as a patron saint
Will soon supplant St. George,
Although the latter dealt in pigs
It’s plain he couldn't forge —
God save the Queen, long may she reign,
By Tory help or Whig—
We’ll never see her like again.
She sent an Irishman to gaol
(The British Lion twirls his tail)
For winking at a pig !
Napier, N.Z.
A. STUART.
The only known instance of a prize-fighter
possessing the least sense of humour was that of
Gunley, an old-time bruiser. Gunley was some-
thing of a runner and cricketer, so he backed him-
self to fight, run and play a one-innings game at
cricket against Gregson, a much larger and cleverer
man with his fists—the winner of two events to
take the money. Gregson merely stipulated that
the fight should come off first, when he intended
to break up his adversary and have a walk-over
for the residue of the match. The ring was
pitched, and a bullet-headed savage, armed with
a borrowed watch, yelled, “Time!” Whereupon
Gunley strolled up to the scratch and fell down,
remarking, “ There, you’ve won that.” He subse-
quently licked Gregson for the remaining items in
their contract, and took the stakes.
The Adelaide folks now complain that
Lady Kintore is “too unassuming.” They can’t
understand a Governor’s wife sitting in a railway
carriage not specially set apart for her.
Adelaide society people read of festivities
at Melbourne and Sydney Government Houses,
but the residence of Lord Kintore is wrapped in a
deep and holy sleep, with ho present signs of
awakening. The only social event during last
week was the prayer-meeting at the local
Y.M.C.A., and even that is becomihg monotonous.
The male and female brick-makers of
Staffordshire have been on strike since December
for an increase of from a penny to twopence a
day. The women moulders, the most skilled
workers, labour from six in the morning to six at
night for a top price of 2s. 6d. for a thousand
bricks with fifty thrown in for “spoils.” Each
brick weighs seven pounds and has to be handled
three times ere finally laid down. That is to say
that in Christian England a woman has to mould
22,050 pounds of clay ere earning Is. 4d.—-for out
of every 30. pence she must give 14 pence to the
female “ page, ” usually a young girl, who carries
tlie moulded bricks to' the kiln. The moulder as
a rule earns (is. Bd. weekly and the “ page ” us. lOd.
Dear Bulletin,—ls it right that human
life should be sacrificed in cold blood for the
benefit of the miserable beings who must have
money, cost what it may? Yet the principle,
devilish as it seems, is at the present time acted
up to on the north-west coast of Australia. It is
a part of that Western Australian pearling-ground
called “The Graveyard !” At this spot the diving
is done in from 19 to 24 fathoms water ; nothing
less than 19. This (as everyone acquainted with
the subject must know) is an extreme depth, even
for the best and hardiest of divers. But “The
Graveyard” is perhaps the richest part of_ the
known shelling-grounds, and so when work is in
full swing it is a very common sight to see a diver
brought up dead—to see Ins dress taken off
in order that one of his countrymen may
literally step into the dead man’s boots! On
some of the boats the black flag is run up so often
that no notice is taken of it, and work goes on
while they have a man whom they can order under
water. One boat-owner lost eight men in about
as many days—but he got a big haul of shell as an
offset! What cares he for the lives of the poor
wretches of divers while his personal gains are
thus added to? He cares not for the coloured
man who earns his profits, and without whom
lie could not take part in the enterprise. To me
this kind of thing resembles murder in the first
degree. I think it is time that some power
stepped in and put a sudden stop to such a cruel
wholesale sacrifice of life. We protect the miner
from the possibilities of death through the careless-
ness or neglect of his masters; why, then, should
we not make it a crime to force divers to work in
a depth of water which means either sudden death
or the lingering illness of a hopeless paralytic ?
Can you imagine anything more callous than to
haul a corpse out of a diving-dress and place a
comrade in it at once, who knows only too well
what has caused death in the first instance, and
the probable fate that awaits him? And, finally
—this as a mere side-issue : Here, in those divers
readiness to go to their death dressed in the
shrouds of the men who have just died —all for
the sake of gain, is a commentary on the non-
deterrent effect exercised by the gallows on those
whom passion or the lust of gain is urging on to
commit murder. — North-West.

The Story of Another Mutiny.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
H.M.S. St, George, 120 guns, Captain
Harry Eyres, 0.8., lay at anchor inside the'break-
water in Plymouth Sound, on the 9th April, 1854,
nearly ready for sea, and under orders to join Sir
Charles Napier’s fleet in the Baltic. But she was
nearly 200 men short of her complement, aild,
having been recently commissioned at Portsmouth,
was ordered on to Plymouth to await the arrival
there of the Portland frigate, the flagship of
Admiral Moresby, on her way home from the
Pacific station to be paid off. War had been
declared with Russia. Two powerful fleets had
sailed —one for the Baltic, the other for the Black
Sea. The retired, reserve, and coast-guard lists
had been taxed to their utmost limits, and still
men were badly wanted. Over 200 of the crew of
the St. George had been drafted from the old
Crocodile, receiving-ship in the Thames; material
out of which to make sailors, perhaps, but for the
time being a contingent of gaol-birds, dead-beats,
and ’longshore loafers. The balance were good
men, mostly long-service pensioners and coast-
guard, thoroughly at home under the pennant, and
anxious for one more slap at an enemy. No
matter who, so long as he possessed that qualifica-
tion.
The Portland arrived in due course. She
had been put in commission at Devonport three
years before; many of her men had their wives
and families ashore—all had friends. But when
the Port Admiral went on board, and they heard
of the declaration of war for the first time, and
were asked for two hundred volunteers for the
St. George, the whole ship’s company responded.
The draft was soon arranged, and final prepara-
tions for sea nearly completed. Liberty boats
landed watch and watch on alternate nights, but
the “Crocodiles,” or “ Tower mob,” as they were
called, were not allowed on shore. Many of them
would never have been seen again. They growled,
showed signs of insubordination, were black-listed
and “hazed” in various ways, and at length
came the climax.
#■ # #
Towards the close of the first watch, and
in response to the demand of Lieutenant Birt-
whistle, the officer of that watch, a small boy in
the undress of a naval cadet crawled out in a
sleepy condition from under the break of the
poop :
“ Youngster, see what time it is. It’s a long
time since six bells was struck.”
“ Aye, aye, sir,” and the lad disappeared down
the after-companion. After an interval of some
minutes seven bells rang out on the keen night
air, but with a peculiarly irregular stroke ; imme-
diately after which he re-appeared, sprang up the
poop-ladder, and reported :
“The clapper of the bell has vanished, sir ! The
sentry has been forrard looking for it for some
minutes. I struck the bell with the galley-poker.
It is getting on for eight bells now.”
“Very good. Go below and report to the com-
mander. Wake him up if he’s asleep.”
In less than five minutes “ Paddy ” May, as he
was called in the gun-room, was on the poop in
conversation with the officer of the watch. Follow-
ing in rapid succession came the orders to “ Pipe
all hands on deck“ A whip on the foreyard,
and get the armourer’s forge up;” “Send the
armourer aft here.” Then the forge was rigged in
the waist of the ship, the fire lit, and the armourer
and His rriate set to work to fashion a new clapper
for the bell, the ’whole f-'hip’s company of over
eight hundred officers and men looking on with an
air of sleepy wonderment. Not a voice was raised
above a whisper. Everybody knew something was
wrong somewhere, but discipline prevented the
gratification of curiosity. When the clapper was
completed and fixed in position, the order was
given to send the forge below, followed by “Pipe
down all hands except the watch on deck. ’ And
by the time three bells in the middle watch was
struck riot a sign or sound remained to suggest
that the ordinary routine o'f duty had been in any
way disturbed.
* *• #
Immediately after “divisions” on the fol-
lowing morning the gunner walked hurriedly aft
with a savage look on his rugged, seamy features
and reported—“ Eighteen tangent-sights gone out
of the middle deck guns, sir, and no trace of
them.’’ Commander May nodded his head in
acknowledgment of the information, quite as if
he had expected it, and went below. At six bells
in the forenoon watch Captain Eyres came on
deck, and again ail hands were turned up. This
time with repeated injunctions to “Lay aft there,
iheri, lay aft, the Captain has something to say to
you.” Standing on the break df the poop the
voice of the grey-haired old sailor was plainly
audible all over the upper deck :
“ Now, men, listen to what I have to say to you
and act upon it. A mutinous spirit has broken
out on board this ship and shown itself in acts of
blackguardism and defiance. Last night the
clapper of the ship’s bell disappeared. This
morning a number of tangent-sights are missing
from the middle-deck guns. I can tell you where
they are. They are among the beef-bones under the
ship’s keel. You must tell me who put them there.
You ‘ Portlands ’ and you petty officers and sea-
men who came round here in the ship from Ports-
mouth, I leave this in yorir hands. We shorten
in cable to-morrow morning at daylight. In a
fortnight’s time we may be under the guns of
Cronstadt. I want men to fight this ship with,
and I know how to deal with those who are not
men. Master-at-arms, see that the mutineers toe
the line there on the quarter-deck when eight bells
strikes in the afternoon watch. Pipe down !”
* * *
At five minutes to 4 o’clock, 14 men were
marched aft by the master-at-arms, and formed
into line between the companion and the gang-
way ; and before the vibrations of the last stroke
of the bell had ceased, the captain, commander,
and first lieutenant came out of the poop-cabin,
Walking slowly along the line, Captain Eyres
scanned the face of each man as he passed, and,
turning to the other officers, remarked
“ Just what I expected. Not a sailor among
the lot. ‘ Crocodiles ’ every man of them.” Then,
turning to his clerk : ‘ ‘ Mr. Luxrnoore, take the
names of these men and make out warrants for
four dozen for each of them. Bring the warrants
to me for signature as soon as ready. Master-at-
arms, see that the prisoners are securely ironed on
the orlop deck, and place a file of marines on
guard over them.”
* * *
With the Bill of Portland just visible
through the morning haze, the last act in the
mutiny on the St. George was consummated, as the
old line-of-battle ship heeled gently over under all
plain sail and top-gallant stunsails before a fresh
south-westerly breeze. All hands were turned up
to witness punishment at five bells, and breakfast
was a trifle later in the wardroom and gun-room
messes that morning in consequence at the un-
usually long programme that liad to be got
through. It fell far short of the prescribed fifty sis
dozen laishosi,- though, for many of the inert fainted
before half the sentence had been carried out, and
were cast loose from the gratings on the report
of the doctor that they were unable to stand more.
Five of them only took the fall dose, brtt not one
of the fourteen evinced any subsequent desire to
again become mutineers. The man who was said
to have been the worst of-the lot and the leader
was shot near Hango Head, in the Gulf of Fin-
land, a few months afterwards, when away in one
of the ship’s boats engaged in the destruction of
dOast signal stations. Another one I met on board
the Arrow, serving the bow Lancaster gun at the
attack on Kertch in the following year. He had
been a London street hawker, arid at odd times a
thief, before hunger and misery drove hifn oil
board the Crocodile. He was then an A. 8., and
pulled stroke oar in the captain’s gig. The mutiny
in Plymouth Sound, ho said, was one of his “ bad
memories. ”

Mr. Justice Pope Cooper, whose wife has
inherited a fortune, will, it is said, go to Europe
and stay there.
Captain Oldham, R.N., objects to certain
questions put to his first-lieutenant by the court-
martial being answered by that officer. Jack
Muck also objects, as far as he dares, to a good
deal that goes on in the same court, but the latter’s
objection doesn’t amount to much on account of
the “five years ” looming in the distance. More-
over, Jack has got some sort of an addled idea
that, as he has to be tried by his officers, the court
should be composed, or partially composed, of
seamen when a charge of tyranny and abuse of
power is brought against an officer. Jack has a
good deal of sense and reason on his side, too, for
if he happens to be not quite as great an ornament
to society as his master, he is generally His equal
in manly attributes, and, almost invariably his
superior in practical sailorising.
An English journal writes : —■
It is said that if the Federation of the Australasian
colonies should become mi fait accompli, the Queen will
grapple the loyal state to the throne with hooks of steel
by nominating the Marquis of Lome Viceroy, and thus
giving her Australasian subjects the distinction of posses-
sing a Royal Vice-Reine. The appointment would be wise
and popular, for Lord Lome is just the solid, able sort of
man that the practical people over there would appreciate
at his proper value, while the beauty and accomplish-
ments of Princess Louise would compel something like
brilliancy in the vice-regal court.
If such things be true how was it that the Lorries
were such social failures in Canada? —how was it
that, instead of grappling that loyal state with
“ hooks of steel,” they only assisted to widen an
ever-growing breach? Probably such an effort
will be made, as The Bulletin predicted, but
that the influence of the sandy-haired, small-
brained son of a Scottish duke and the cold, pom-
pous, haughty, disappointed daughter of two
commonplace German nobodies can turn away a
nation from the fulfilment of its obvious national
destiny is fudge.

A Mississippi paper thus advertised a sow
for sale : “Bold for no fault except that, having
been raised by a preacher, she acquired too great
a partiality for spring chicken to be just agreeable
to the present owner.”
One of The Bulletin’s Melbourne men
has had a chat with James M , who spent 40
years in trade as a professional burglar, and passed
a fortnight on the hulk Success shortly after
Price’s murder. James has been a respectable
man for many years, and is now 84; he can be
seen on the sunny side of Bourke-street any after-
noon. He says that the most noxious man about
Price was “ a beastly little Tasmanian detective ”
he brought with him named P . This man
evidently gave Marcus Clarke some idea of the
sneak, Troke. Dr. Youl advised Price always to
carry loaded pistols, but this official desperado
had such confidence in his invulnerability that'he
laughed it to scorn. The Success men used to go
ashore and work, and only went aboard to sleep.
M—says that Supt. Dwyer, now of Pentridge,
once left the force through Price’s inhumanity.
A Melbourne reporter- —let us call him
Squills—owns to a strange fascination over the
performances of Pattie Browne, because he was
within an ace of. making a frightful mistake
over her once—the kind of thing that happens
at some time to every journalist, like the hay-
fever, or writing a tragedy. Another Batty
Brown, quite different in appearance, came before
the City Police Court, and was said to have been
a stage super. Squills had never seen Pattie on
the stage, to take particular notice, and the
attorney for the defence of the other Patty quite
innocently mixed up the two careers, the conse-
quence being that Squills got a magnificently-
moral paragraph in type. Pattie Browne was
acting that night at the Theatre Royal, in
“ Siberia,” and the sight of a play-bill in the
window of the Royal Mail Hotel was the trifling
circumstance that led Squills to get the magni-
ficent paragraph smashed beforehand.

HE THREE GREAT LIGHTS!
The Needles Patent Gas-making Machine;
The Harvey Patent Safety Kerosene Lamp;
And the New Era Portable Gas Lamp.
The Harvey Lamp has been adopted by the
Railway Commissioners for lighting the Sydney
trains. We were awarded the Highest Order of
Merit and Special Mention by experts at the
late Melbourne Exhibition for our lighting
mediums. Over 6000 Harvey Lamps are in use
in this colony, and for all lighting purposes are
giving universal satisfaction. These Lamps can
be seen on one of the North' Shore ferry-boats,
and are suitable for all classes of vessels. The
Inventor offers £200 to anyone producing as good
a Kerosene Burner.
